Nafaqah — Maintenance

Definition and Obligation of Nafaqah for the Wife
Defining nafaqah and stating its obligation on the husband for the wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of nafaqah, identify its three components, and explain why it is wajib on the husband for his wife.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is the wajib duty to provide food, clothing, and housing, established by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

In this lesson, we explore the opening of the chapter on nafaqah from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The author first clarifies the linguistic meaning, then gives the Shari'ah definition: nafaqah is food, clothing, and housing. The obligation arises from marriage, kinship, or ownership; here we focus on the marriage cause. The proofs are strong and universally accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النفقة في اللغة الإنسان على عياله ونحو ذلك قال تعالى وما منعهم أن تقبل منهم نفقاتهم ﴾ [التوبة : ٥٤] ويقال أنفق الرجل من النفقة قال تعالى لينفق ذو سعة من سخته كره ای [الطلاق: ۷] وأنفق القوم إذا أنفقت سوقهم، وأنفق الرجل إذا ذهب ماله ويقال منه قوله تعالى إذاً لأمسكتم خشية الإنفاق [الإسراء: ۱۰۰] أي خشية الفقر. ويقال نفقت السلعة نفاقاً نقيض كسدت ونفقة الدابة تفوقاً إذا ماتت ؛ كذا في ضياء الحلوم. وبه علم أن النفقة المرادة هنا ليست مشتقة من النفوق بمعنى الهلاك، ولا من النفق ولا من النفاق، بل هي اسم للشيء الذي ينفقه الرجل على عياله. وأما في الشريعة فذكر في الخلاصة قال هشام : سألت محمداً . النفقة قال : قالوا: ونفقة الغير النفقة هي الطعام والكسوة والسكنى ا هـ . تجب على الغير بأسباب ثلاثة : بالزوجية والقرابة .والملك فبدأ بالأول لمناسبة ما تقدم من النكاح والطلاق والعدة. قوله : (تجب النفقة للزوجة على زوجها والكسوة بقدر (حالهما) أي الطعام والشراب بقرينة عطف الكسوة والسكنى عليها ،والأصل في ذلك قوله تعالى لينفق ذو سعة من سعته [الطلاق: ۷] وقوله تعالى وعلى المولود له رزقهن وكسوتهن بالمعروف [البقرة: ٢٣٣] وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام في حجة الوداع ولهن عليكم رزقهن وكسوتهن بالمعروف» وعليه إجماع الأمة، لأن النفقة جزاء الاحتباس فكل من كان محبوساً بحق مقصود لغيره كانت
Chapter of Nafaqah. In language, [it means] the human [spends] on his dependents and the like. Allah said: 'And what prevented them that their expenditures should be accepted from them?' (9:54). It is said: 'the man spent' from nafaqah. Allah said: 'Let the wealthy man spend from his wealth' (65:7). And 'the people spent' when their market [goods] were spent. And 'the man spent' when his wealth went away. From it is His saying: 'Then you would withhold for fear of spending' (17:100), i.e., fear of poverty. It is said: 'the commodity sold' as opposite of 'stagnated', and 'the animal died' (by nafaqah). Thus in Diya' al-Hulum. By this it is known that the intended nafaqah here is not derived from nafaq (death), nor from nafaq (tunnel), nor from hypocrisy, but it is the name for the thing that a man spends on his dependents. As for in Shari'ah: It is mentioned in al-Khulasah: Hisham said: I asked Muhammad about nafaqah. He said: They said: And the nafaqah of others: nafaqah is food, clothing, and housing. It is obligatory on others by three causes: by marriage, by kinship, and by ownership. So he began with the first due to its relevance to what preceded of marriage, divorce, and waiting period. His statement: (Nafaqah is obligatory for the wife on her husband, and clothing according to their status) i.e., food and drink, by the context of mentioning clothing and housing alongside them. The basis for this is Allah's statement: 'Let the wealthy man spend from his wealth' (65:7), and His statement: 'And for the father, their provisions and clothing are due in accordance with what is acceptable' (2:233), and his statement, peace be upon him, in the Farewell Pilgrimage: 'And upon you is their provision and clothing in accordance with what is acceptable.' And there is consensus of the Ummah on this, because nafaqah is the recompense for confinement; so whoever is confined by a right intended for another, it is [obligatory].
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement from the beginning of Bab al-Nafaqah. It first provides the linguistic and legal definitions of nafaqah, clarifying that in fiqh it means food, clothing, and housing. The author then states that nafaqah becomes obligatory upon others (the husband, relatives, and owner) due to three causes: marriage, kinship, and ownership. The focus here is on the first cause: marriage. The obligation is established by Quran (65:7, 2:233), the Prophet's hadith in the Farewell Pilgrimage, and consensus. The commentary explains that nafaqah is the compensation (jaza') for the wife's confinement (ihtibas) in marriage; since the husband has the right to detain her, he must provide for her.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance: food, clothing, and housing for the wife.
كسوة (kiswah): Clothing, to be provided according to the status of both spouses.
سكنى (sukna): Housing, a component of nafaqah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rationale (illah) for the obligation of nafaqah on the husband?
A) Because the wife is poor
B) Because the wife is confined for the husband's right
C) Because the husband is wealthy
D) Because the wife provides services

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah is an immediate wajib on the husband upon marriage, consisting of food, clothing, and housing, based on clear Quranic and prophetic proofs and consensus.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Ownership of Fixed Maintenance and the Husband's Spending Role
Explaining that fixed maintenance becomes tamlik but husband manages spending

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that fixed maintenance is tamlik (ownership) not ibaha, explain the husband's duty to spend as qawwam, and apply the ruling to surplus and charity cases.

Core Idea
Maintenance fixed by judge or given by husband becomes wife's property, but husband spends it as her guardian.

In Hanafi law, maintenance (nafaqa) is an obligation on the husband. However, what is the legal nature of the money once fixed? Is it simply a permission to use, or does it become the wife's property? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the important distinction between tamlik and ibaha, and explains how the husband's role as qawwam impacts spending, surplus, and the wife's right to charity or reimbursement.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish tamlik from ibaha
Identify the evidence from al-Qunyah: if the husband says 'Take these for your maintenance' without specifying time, it is tamlik (ownership), not ibaha (permission). Fixing by the judge also creates ownership, as per al-Dhakhira.
Tamlik transfers ownership; ibaha only permits use.
2
Analyze the effect of judicial fixing
The judge's fixing transfers ownership to the wife. Proof: if spouses reconcile on a sum unsuitable as an estimate, it becomes a compensatory transaction, which would only be valid if she owned the fixed amount.
Each month is treated independently; surplus does not offset future fixing.
3
Determine the husband's spending role
Despite ownership, the husband is the spender (yali al-infaq) unless he delays. This is because he is qawwam, not because he may take surplus.
If husband delays, judge may order payment to wife; himl is imprisoned but obligation remains.
4
Apply to surplus and charity
The wife owns the surplus; she may keep it, give charity, or feed others. The husband cannot take it. If she overspends before month ends, no new fixing for that period.
This confirms ownership, not mere permission.
5
Note the exception: wife’s own spending
If wife spent her own money or from begging, she may reclaim from husband (al-Khaniyya).
The husband remains ultimately liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة لو فرض لها القاضي عشرة دراهم نفقة شهر فمضى الشهر وقد بقي من العشرة شيء يفرض لها القاضي عشرة أخرى، وفرق النفقة الكسوة كما سنبينه في الكسوة. ويدل عليه أيضاً ما فيها : أنهما لو اصطلحا بعد فرض النفقة على شيء لا يصلح تقديراً للنفقة كان معاوضة كالعبد فلولا أنها ملكت النفقة المفروضة لما كان معاوضة. وفي القنية : قال لها خذي هذه الدنانير الخمسة لنفقتك ولم يعين الوقت فهو تمليك لا إباحة ا.هـ. فيفيد أنها تملك النفقة بفرض القاضي أو بدفع شيء بالرضا لكن في الخلاصة والذخيرة إذا فرض القاضي النفقة فالزوج هو الذي يلي الإنفاق إلا إذا ظهر عند القاضي مطله فحينئذ يفرض النفقة ويأمره ليعطيها لتنفق على نفسها نظراً لها فإن لم يعط حبسه ولا تسقط عنه النفقة ا هـ . فهي وإن ملكتها بالفرض لم تتصرف فيها بالإنفاق كما في الخلاصة. وتفرع على هذا ما لو قرر لها كل يوم مثلاً قدراً معيناً من النفقة فأمرته بإنفاق البعض وأرادت أن تمسك الباقي فمقتضى التمليك أن لها ذلك كما تقدم التصريح به عن الذخيرة في نفقة الشهر، ولا فرق بين نفقة شهر أو يوم فليس فائدة أنه يلي الإنفاق مع فرض القاضي إلا لكونه قواماً عليها لا لأنه يأخذ ما فضل. وعلى هذا لو أمرأته بشراء طعام فاشترى لها فأكلت وفضل شيء واستغنت عنه في يومها فليس له أكله والتصرف فيه إليها هو مقتضى التمليك. ويدل عليه أيضاً أنها لو أسرفت في نفقة الشهر فأكلتها قبل مضيه واحتاجت لا يفرض لها أخرى كما لو هلكت كما في الذخيرة. فالحاصل أن المفروضة أو المدفوعة إليها ملك لها فلها الإطعام منها والتصدق. وفي الخانية : المرأة إذا فرضت لها النفقة من مال نفسها أو من مسألة الناس كان لها أن ترجع بالمفروض على زوجها ا هـ . قوله : (فهي وإن ملكتها بالفرض لم تتصرف) أي ليس لها التصرف فيها بالإنفاق وإنما الإنفاق له
And in al-Dhakhira: If the judge fixed for her ten dirhams as maintenance for a month, and the month passed and something remained from the ten, the judge fixes another ten for her. And the difference between maintenance and clothing will be explained where we discuss clothing. And also indicated by what is in it: if they reconcile after the fixing of maintenance on something that is not suitable as an estimate for maintenance, it is a compensation like a slave; if it were not that she owned the fixed maintenance, it would not be compensation. And in al-Qunyah: He said to her, 'Take these five dinars for your maintenance' and he did not specify the time, so it is tamlik (ownership) not ibaha (permission). End. So it indicates that she owns the maintenance by the judge's fixing or by paying something with mutual consent. But in al-Khulasa and al-Dhakhira: When the judge fixes maintenance, the husband is the one who undertakes spending, unless it appears to the judge that he is delaying; then the judge fixes maintenance and orders him to give it to her so she can spend on herself out of consideration for her. If he does not give, he is imprisoned and the maintenance does not drop from him. End. So she, even if she owns it by the fixing, does not dispose of it in spending. And branching from this: If he allocated for her, for example, a specific amount of maintenance each day, and she ordered him to spend some and wanted to keep the rest, then the implication of tamlik is that she has that right, as was explicitly stated from al-Dhakhira regarding monthly maintenance. And there is no difference between monthly maintenance or daily. So there is no benefit that he undertakes spending with the judge's fixing except because he is her guardian (qawwam), not because he takes what remains. And accordingly, if she ordered him to buy food, and he bought for her, and she ate and something remained and she became independent of it that day, then it is not permissible for him to eat it; the disposal of it belongs to her, which is the implication of tamlik. And also indicated by that: if she overspent on the monthly maintenance and ate it before it passed and needed [more], no other maintenance is fixed for her, just as if it had perished, as in al-Dhakhira. So the conclusion: what is fixed or paid to her is her property; she may feed others from it and give charity. And in al-Khaniyya: If maintenance was fixed for a woman from her own wealth or from begging from people, she may claim back what was fixed from her husband. End. His statement: ('So she, even if she owns it by the fixing, does not dispose') means she has no right to dispose of it in spending; rather spending is for him.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school regarding the nature of fixed maintenance (nafaqa) given to a wife. It begins by citing al-Dhakhira: if a judge fixes a monthly maintenance of ten dirhams and the month passes with a surplus, the judge fixes another ten for the next month, treating each month independently. The author then distinguishes maintenance from clothing, which will be explained later. A proof from al-Dhakhira is given: if the spouses reconcile after a fixing on a sum that is unsuitable as an estimate, it becomes a compensatory transaction (mu'awada) like selling a slave, indicating that the fixed maintenance becomes owned by the wife. Otherwise it would not be a valid compensation. Next, al-Qunyah is quoted: if the husband says 'take these five dinars for your maintenance' without specifying a time, it is tamlik (ownership) not ibaha (permission), meaning the wife owns the money. However, the author then clarifies from al-Khulasa and al-Dhakhira that although the wife owns the fixed maintenance, the husband is the one who actually spends it in kind or cash on her needs, unless he is delaying (mutil), in which case the judge may order him to give it to her so she can spend it herself. If he still refuses, he may be imprisoned, but the maintenance obligation does not drop. Thus, ownership does not entail the right to spend; the husband remains the spender. A branch ruling (tafri') follows: if the husband assigns a daily amount and the wife asks him to spend part and keep the rest, she may keep it because she owns it. The fact that the husband is the spender is due to his role as qawwam (guardian), not because he can take the surplus. Similarly, if she orders him to buy food and there is leftover, the leftover is hers; he may not eat it. Another indication: if she overspends her monthly maintenance before the month ends and then needs more, the judge will not fix more, just as if the money had been lost. The conclusion is that fixed or paid maintenance is the wife's property; she may feed others or give charity from it. Finally, al-Khaniyya adds: if the wife had maintenance from her own wealth or by begging (i.e., she spent her own money or that given by others), she may claim reimbursement from her husband. The commentator (possibly al-Ramli) concludes by confirming the ruling: 'she does not have the right to dispose of it in spending; spending is for the husband.' This passage draws on authoritative sources: al-Dhakhira, al-Qunyah, al-Khulasa, and al-Khaniyya, which are part of the Hanafi corpus. The ruling is consistent with the principle that maintenance is an obligation on the husband, and the wife's ownership of the specific amount given does not override his duty to provide directly.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; the wife becomes owner of the sum fixed or given as maintenance.
إباحة (ibaha): Permission; the wife is allowed to use the money but does not own it.
قوام (qawwam): Guardian or manager; the husband's role as the one who oversees spending for the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wife overspends her monthly maintenance before the month ends and then needs more, what is the ruling according to al-Dhakhira?
A) The judge fixes another maintenance for the remainder of the month.
B) No additional maintenance is fixed for that month.
C) The husband must pay extra from his own pocket.
D) The wife must borrow and repay later.

Key Takeaway
Fixed maintenance becomes the wife's property (tamlik), but the husband retains the duty to spend it as her guardian (qawwam); the wife owns the surplus and may give charity, but the husband cannot take it.

Judge's Fixing of Maintenance and the Dispute on Taking a Surety
Detailing when and how a judge fixes maintenance and the ikhtilaf on surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute regarding whether a judge can force a husband to provide a surety for future maintenance, and state the conditions required for the judge's fixing.

Core Idea
A judge's fixing of maintenance is qada requiring the husband's presence; a surety for future maintenance is only permitted by istihsan per Abu Yusuf.

This passage from al-Bada'i al-Sana'i details the conditions under which a judge may fix maintenance for a wife and presents a dispute between the founding imams of the Hanafi school. The central question is whether the judge can force the husband to provide a surety when the wife fears his absence. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind each position and the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on surety
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa: the judge does not force the husband to give a surety, even if the wife fears absence.
This is based on the principle that the judge's fixing is qada, and a surety is not part of the original fixing.
2
Identify the opposing view
Note that Abu Yusuf (the companion) holds a different view via istihsan: the judge may take a surety for one month's maintenance.
The approval of the surety is a concession (rukhsa) based on the wife's need.
3
Analyse the opposing proof
Understand Abu Yusuf's dalil: istihsan, which gives preference to a stronger need (the wife's fear of absence) over the general rule that qada does not impose a surety.
Istihsan is a valid Hanafi source of law.
4
Determine the Hanafi response
The text does not give a direct rebuttal, but notes that the fatwa (mufta bihi) according to al-Walwaljiyya is on Abu Yusuf's opinion.
Later Hanafi scholars adopted Abu Yusuf's view as operative.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the wife cannot obtain a surety; under Abu Yusuf, she can obtain a surety for one month, giving her security.
This affects the judge's decision in court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البدائع : : وإذا طلبت المرأة من القاضي فرض النفقة قبل النقلة وهي بحيث لا تمتنع من التسليم لو طالبها بالتسليم أو كان امتناعها لحق فرض القاضي لها إعانة لها على الوصول إلى حقها الواجب، وإن كان بعدما حولها إلى منزله فزعمت عدم الإنفاق أو التضييق فلا ينبغي له أن يعجل بالفرض ولكن يأمره بالنفقة والتوسيع إلى أن يظهر ظلمه، فحينئذ يفرض عليه النفقة ويأمره أن يدفعها إليها لتنفق على نفسها ولو طلبت كفيلاً بها خوفاً من غيبته لا يجبره القاضي على إعطاء الكفيل عند أبي حنيفة، واستحسن أبو يوسف أخذ كفيل بنفقة شهر . ويشترط لوجوب الفرض على القاضي وجوازه منه شرطان أحدهما طلب المرأة، والثاني حضرة الزوج حتى لو كان الزوج غائباً فطلبت المرأة من القاضي فرض نفقة عليه لم يفرض وإن كان عالماً بالزوجية عند أبي حنيفة في قوله الأخير لأن الفرض من القاضي قضاء وقد صح من أصلنا أن القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز من غير خصم، وقوله عليه السلام لامرأة أبي سفيان كان على سبيل الفتوى لا على طريق القضاء بدليل أنه لم يقدر لها ما تأخذه، وفرض من القاضي تقديرها، فإذا لم تقدر لم تكن فرضاً فلم تكن قضاء وسيأتي تمامه فيما إذا غاب وله مال عند مودعه وفي الولوالجية : الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف في أخذ الكفيل بنفقة شهر.
And in al-Bada'i: When a woman asks the judge to fix maintenance before moving [to the husband's house], and she is in a state where she would not refuse to submit if he demanded submission, or her refusal is due to a right, the judge's fixing for her is an aid for her to reach her due right. But if it is after he moved her to his home and she claims lack of spending or restriction, then he should not hasten to fix, but rather order him to spend and be generous until his oppression appears. Then he fixes maintenance and orders him to pay it to her so she can spend on herself. If she asks for a surety for it out of fear of his absence, the judge does not force him to give a surety according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf adopted istihsan and takes a surety for one month's maintenance. Two conditions are required for the obligation and validity of the fixing by the judge: first, the woman's request; second, the presence of the husband. So if the husband is absent and the woman asks the judge to fix maintenance on him, he does not fix it even if he knows of the marriage according to Abu Hanifa in his later opinion, because the fixing by the judge is qada (judgment), and it is established in our school that judgment on an absentee is not valid without a representative. And his statement (peace be upon him) to the wife of Abu Sufyan was on the basis of ifta (legal opinion) not qada, as evidence that he did not estimate for her what she should take. And the fixing by the judge is its estimation; if it is not estimated, it is not a fixing, so it is not qada. And its completion will come regarding when he is absent and has wealth with his depositary. And in al-Walwaljiyya: The fatwa is on the opinion of Abu Yusuf in taking a surety for one month's maintenance.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage from al-Bada'i al-Sana'i explains the judge's role in fixing maintenance (nafaqa) for a wife. It distinguishes two situations: before the wife moves to the husband's home, the judge helps her obtain her due right by fixing maintenance if she would not refuse to submit to the husband. After she moves, the judge does not immediately fix maintenance; instead, he orders the husband to spend freely until the husband's oppression is evident. At that point, the judge fixes and orders the husband to pay to her. The passage then presents an intra-madhhab dispute: if the wife asks for a surety (kafil) for future maintenance out of fear of the husband's absence, Abu Hanifa holds that the judge may not force the husband to give a surety, while Abu Yusuf uses istihsan to permit taking a surety for one month. The fatwa according to al-Walwaljiyya is on Abu Yusuf's opinion. Two conditions are essential for the judge's fixing: the wife's request and the husband's presence. If the husband is absent, the judge cannot fix maintenance even if he knows the marriage, because the fixing is a judicial decree (qada), and Hanafi usul does not allow judgment against an absent party without a representative. The Prophet's hadith to Hind (wife of Abu Sufyan) is cited as evidence: the Prophet gave a legal opinion (ifta) not a judgment, because he did not specify a particular amount to take. Since the judge's fixing involves estimation (taqdir), if no estimation occurs, it is not qada. The matter will be further discussed regarding an absent husband who has wealth deposited. This passage combines a ruling (hukm), proof from sunnah, and a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, making it an inter-madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Judicial decree; a binding judgment by a judge that requires both parties to be present.
فتوى (fatwa): Legal opinion; not a binding judgment, as given by the Prophet to Hind.
كفيل (kafil): Surety or guarantor; someone who guarantees payment of maintenance.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that sets aside strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable solution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa's later opinion, why does the judge not fix maintenance if the husband is absent?
A) Because the wife's request is not valid without the husband.
B) Because the fixing is qada, and judgment on an absentee is not allowed without a representative.
C) Because maintenance is only fixed when the husband is present.
D) Because the Prophet's hadith prohibits fixing in absence.

Key Takeaway
The judge's fixing of maintenance is qada requiring the husband's presence; the issue of a surety for future maintenance involves a valid ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school, with the mufta bihi being the permission by istihsan.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Quantum of Maintenance: No Fixed Amount — Based on Sufficiency and Custom
Establishing that no fixed amount exists; maintenance is determined by sufficiency and custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that maintenance has no fixed amount and explain how the hadith of Hind supports determination by sufficiency and custom.

Core Idea
Maintenance has no fixed amount; it is based on sufficiency (kifaya) and custom (maruf).

In this passage, the commentator clarifies why the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq did not specify a fixed amount for maintenance: because the purpose is sufficiency, which varies by person, time, and place. The judge must assess what is sufficient according to local custom, following the hadith of Hind where the Prophet said, 'Take what suffices you and your child in fairness.'

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school does not fix a specific amount for maintenance; it is wajib based on sufficiency (kifaya) and referred to custom (maruf).
The author mentions earlier sources like al-Dhakhira and al-Bada'i to support this.
2
Locate the proof
Find the primary evidence: the hadith of Hind bint Utbah, recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim, where the Prophet said, 'ما يكفيك وولدك بالمعروف' (what suffices you and your child in fairness).
This hadith is cited in Fath al-Qadir as the basis.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
Examine the two key terms: 'الكفاية' (sufficiency) indicates that the amount is not fixed but must meet needs; 'المعروف' (fairness/custom) indicates that the judge must refer to local norms and market prices.
The term 'بالمعروف' is the basis for relying on urf (custom).
4
Apply the ruling
Understand that when a judge fixes maintenance today, he must look at market prices and what is customary for the couple's social class, not apply a historical figure like the four dirhams mentioned by Imam Muhammad.
The four dirhams was an example for his time, not a binding rule.

And the author (of Kanz al-Daqaiq) did not mention a fixed amount for maintenance because of what is in al-Dhakhira and others: that in our school there is no fixed necessary amount for maintenance, because the purpose of maintenance is sufficiency (kifaya), and that varies with people's natures and conditions, and also varies with different times. So fixing a specific amount would cause harm to one of them. What he said in the book: 'If the husband is poor, the judge fixes four dirhams for her' is not a fixed necessary amount; rather Muhammad (al-Shaybani) estimated it based on what he saw in his time. So what is right for the judge in our time is to consider sufficiency according to custom (maruf). The basis of that is the hadith of Hind, where he considered sufficiency. And in al-Bada'i: When its obligation is based on sufficiency, the husband must provide what suffices her of food, condiment (idam), and oil, because bread is not usually eaten without condiment. As for oil, it is indispensable for women. Similar [ruling] applies to both. End. And in al-Dhakhira: They said: Meat is not part of condiment, especially according to Abu Hanifa's principle regarding oaths. So see: If the woman is extremely wealthy, eating sweets and the like, and the husband is likewise, then the judge imposes on him the standard of the middle class. So according to what they use as condiment in their custom, that is imposed on the husband. And in judicial practice, condiment is also imposed: its highest is meat, its lowest is oil, and its middle is milk. And it is said regarding a poor woman: no condiment is imposed unless it is barley bread. And in Fath al-Qadir: The truth is to refer to their custom. End. And in al-Mujtaba: Maintenance is bread, meat, oil for the head, lamp oil, the price of water, and some kind of fruit. For the poor husband, of food: barley bread if that is the food of their poor, and ten ustars of meat, and five ustars of fat and suet, and no fruit for her. End. The conclusion: The judge, when he intends to fix maintenance, should look at the price in the city and what suffices her. So it is referred to their custom. And in Fath al-Qadir, citing the two Sahihs: Hind bint Utbah said, 'O Messenger of Allah, Abu Sufyan is a stingy man who does not give me what suffices me and my child, except what I take from
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage addresses the quantum of maintenance (nafaqa) in Hanafi fiqh. The author (likely al-Bahr al-Raiq commenting on Kanz al-Daqaiq) explains why the original matn did not specify a fixed amount: because maintenance is based on sufficiency (kifaya), which varies by person, time, and custom. The mention of four dirhams for a poor husband in the matn is not a fixed ruling but merely an example from Imam Muhammad's time. The judge must assess what is sufficient according to urban custom (maruf). The primary evidence is the hadith of Hind, where the Prophet ordered her to take 'what suffices you and your child in fairness' (ما يكفيك وولدك بالمعروف). The passage then details the components of maintenance from various sources: al-Bada'i states that the husband must provide food (khubz), condiment (idam), and oil (duhn), because bread is not eaten alone, and oil is necessary for women. Al-Dhakhira discusses whether meat is considered condiment; according to Abu Hanifa's principle in oaths, meat is not condiment, but in practice the judge considers the couple's custom. Different levels of condiment are prescribed: highest meat, middle milk, lowest oil. For a poor wife, condiment may not be imposed unless barley bread is the norm. Al-Mujtaba provides a detailed list: bread, meat, oil for hair, lamp oil, water price, and fruit. For a poor husband, barley bread, ten ustars of meat, five ustars of fat, and no fruit. Ultimately, the judge must look at market prices and what suffices the wife, referring to custom. The hadith of Hind from al-Bukhari and Muslim is the basis: the wife may take from the husband's wealth what is customary for her and her child's needs. This passage integrates multiple sources from the Hanafi corpus: al-Dhakhira, al-Bada'i, al-Mujtaba, Fath al-Qadir, and the Sahihayn, demonstrating the flexibility of maintenance rulings.

Key Terms
الكفاية (al-kifaya): Sufficiency; the amount that meets the wife's needs without excess or deficiency.
الإدام (al-idam): Condiment; food eaten with bread, such as meat, oil, or milk.
المعروف (al-maruf): What is customary and fair; the recognized norm in a given society.

C) Ijma (consensus) of the Companions.
D) Qiyas on the maintenance of slaves.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance in the Hanafi school is determined by sufficiency (kifaya) and custom (maruf), not by any fixed amount; the judge must assess based on market and social norms.

Conditions for the Judge to Impose Maintenance
Sets forth two conditions for the judge to impose maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a judge to impose maintenance and explain why the husband's presence is required, using the Hanafi distinction between qada and fatwa.

Core Idea
A judge cannot impose maintenance on an absent husband because it is a judicial decree requiring both parties present.

This lesson examines the procedural conditions for a judge to impose spousal maintenance. The Hanafi school sets strict requirements to ensure fairness: the wife must request it and the husband must be present. We will explore the evidence from the Prophet's interaction with Hind and the intra-Hanafi difference on taking a guarantor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Read the passage to find the two conditions required for a judge to impose maintenance. Note that both must be present for the judge to act.
The conditions are the wife's request and the husband's presence.
2
Understand why absence prevents imposition
The author states that the judge's imposition is a judicial decree (qada), and in Hanafi usul, qada cannot be passed against an absent party without an adversary. This is the key legal principle.
The judge's act is qada, not fatwa; fatwa does not require presence.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
The Prophet's statement to Hind is cited. Why is it a fatwa and not a qada? Because the Prophet did not estimate a specific amount; estimation is required for qada. Thus, a judge must estimate, and without estimation, it is not a qada.
The lack of estimation is the key indicator.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text mentions Abu Yusuf's view allowing a guarantor for one month's maintenance. Understand the difference and which opinion is relied upon for fatwa.
Fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view per al-Walwalijiyya.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi is the opinion of Abu Yusuf: a guarantor can be taken for one month. This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.
The earlier position of Abu Hanifa is not the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشترط لوجوب الفرض على القاضي وجوازه منه شرطان أحدهما طلب المرأة، والثاني حضرة الزوج حتى لو كان الزوج غائباً فطلبت المرأة من القاضي فرض نفقة عليه لم يفرض وإن كان عالماً بالزوجية عند أبي حنيفة في قوله الأخير لأن الفرض من القاضي قضاء وقد صح من أصلنا أن القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز من غير خصم، وقوله عليه السلام لامرأة أبي سفيان كان على سبيل الفتوى لا على طريق القضاء بدليل أنه لم يقدر لها ما تأخذه، وفرض من القاضي تقديرها، فإذا لم تقدر لم تكن فرضاً فلم تكن قضاء وسيأتي تمامه فيما إذا غاب وله مال عند مودعه وفي الولوالجية : الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف في أخذ الكفيل بنفقة شهر.
Two conditions are required for the obligation of the judge's imposition and its permissibility from him: one is the woman's request, and the second is the presence of the husband. So that if the husband is absent and the woman asks the judge to impose maintenance on him, he does not impose it, even if he knows of the marriage, according to Abu Hanifa in his later opinion, because the judge's imposition is a judicial decree, and it is established in our principle that judgment against an absent party is not permissible without an adversary. And the Prophet's statement to the wife of Abu Sufyan was by way of fatwa, not by way of qada, as evidenced by the fact that he did not estimate for her what she should take, and the judge's imposition requires estimation; so if he did not estimate, it was not an imposition, so it was not a judgment. And its completion will come later regarding when he is absent and has property with someone who is his depositary. And in al-Walwalijiyya: The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's opinion regarding taking a guarantor for one month's maintenance.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq states the procedural conditions for a judge to impose maintenance. The author identifies two conditions: (1) the wife must request it, and (2) the husband must be present. If the husband is absent, the judge cannot impose maintenance even if he knows of the marriage, according to Abu Hanifa's later position. The reason is that the judge's imposition is a judicial decree (qada), and in the Hanafi school, a judgment cannot be passed against an absent party without an opponent. The Prophet's statement to Hind (the wife of Abu Sufyan) is cited as evidence: the Prophet did not specify an amount, indicating it was a fatwa (legal opinion) rather than a judicial ruling, since a judge's imposition requires estimation of the amount. The text notes that Abu Yusuf's view on taking a guarantor for one month's maintenance is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, financial support for the wife including food, clothing, housing, etc.
فرض (fard): In this context, the judge's imposition of a specific amount of maintenance.
قضاء (qada): A judicial decree, a binding judgment by a judge.
فتوى (fatwa): A non-binding legal opinion, as opposed to a binding judicial decree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A judge cannot impose maintenance on an absent husband because it is a judicial decree requiring both parties present, but the mufta bihi opinion allows taking a guarantor for one month.

Maintenance Based on Sufficiency, Not Fixed Amount
Establishes that maintenance has no fixed amount but is based on sufficiency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on maintenance amount and apply the principle of sufficiency (kifaya) to determine the wife's entitlement.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school bases maintenance on sufficiency (kifaya), not a fixed amount, as proven by the hadith of Hind.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies why the author of al-Kanz did not specify a fixed amount for maintenance. It establishes that the obligation is to provide what suffices the wife according to her needs and local custom, varying by time and place. The proof is the Prophet's instruction to Hind to take what suffices, without fixing an amount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Recognize that maintenance (nafaqah) is wajib, but its amount is not fixed; the purpose is sufficiency (kifaya). The judge must determine the amount based on local custom and prices.
This principle is derived from the hadith of Hind and the varying nature of people's needs.
2
Understand the rationale against fixed estimates
Why did the author of al-Kanz not mention an estimate? Because sufficiency varies by people's circumstances and times; a fixed amount would harm one party. Judge must consider current realities.
Imam Muhammad's four dirhams was a historical observation, not a binding rule.
3
Analyze the proof from the hadith of Hind
The Prophet told Hind: 'Take what suffices you and your child with custom.' No amount was specified, indicating the standard is sufficiency, not a fixed number.
The hadith is zanni in transmission (ahad) but clear in indicating the method.
4
Apply the principle to determine sufficiency
The judge must look at the wife's status (wealthy, middle, poor) and local customs for food, condiment, oil, etc. The goal is what meets her basic needs without excess.
If the wife is poor, barley bread and basic condiment suffices; if wealthy, higher standard product.
5
Implement the ruling via judge's estimation
The judge should check market prices, estimate the cost of sufficient items in dirhams, and impose a monetary maintenance. This is practical for enforcement.
The judge's assessment is not arbitrary; it must be based on evidence and custom.

Source Text

The author did not mention a fixed estimate for maintenance because, as mentioned in al-Dhakhira and others, according to us maintenance has no necessary fixed estimate because the purpose of maintenance is sufficiency, and that varies with people's natures and circumstances and also varies with different times. So fixing a specific amount causes harm to one of them. What was mentioned in the book (al-Kanz) that if the husband is poor, the judge imposes four dirhams – that is not a necessary fixed estimate, but rather Muhammad (al-Shaybani) estimated it based on what he observed in his time. So what is right for the judge in our time is to consider sufficiency according to custom. Its origin is the hadith of Hind, where he considered sufficiency. In al-Bada'i: When its obligation is based on sufficiency, then the husband must provide what suffices her of food, condiments, and oil, because bread is not usually eaten without condiment, and as for oil, women cannot do without it. Similarly, if they are both... etc. In al-Dhakhira they said: Meat is not considered a condiment, especially according to Abu Hanifa's principle in oaths. So if the woman is extremely wealthy and eats sweets and the like, and the husband is likewise, then the average of the people is imposed on him; so based on what they habitually use as condiment, it is imposed on the husband. In al-Aqdiya, condiment is also imposed: its highest is meat, its lowest is oil, and its middle is milk. It was said for the poor woman, no condiment is imposed unless he has barley bread. In Fath al-Qadir: The truth is to refer that to their custom. In al-Mujtaba: Maintenance includes bread, meat, oil for the head, oil for the lamp, price of water, and some fruit. For the poor husband: barley bread if that is the food of their poor, ten uqiyas of meat, five uqiyas of fat and tail, and no fruit for her. The conclusion is that it is appropriate for the judge, when he wants to impose maintenance, to look at the prices of the town and consider what suffices her according to the custom of that town, and estimate the items in dirhams, then impose in dirhams, as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage explains that maintenance in the Hanafi school has no fixed amount, contrary to what some might think from Imam Muhammad's example of four dirhams. The basis is sufficiency (kifaya), which varies by person, circumstance, and time. The judge must consider the local cost of living and customs. The text discusses various opinions on what constitutes sufficiency, including food items like bread, condiments (idam), oil, meat, etc. The conclusion is that the judge should estimate based on current prices and impose a monetary amount. This is a ruling_with_proof, with the hadith of Hind as the proof that the Prophet considered sufficiency without fixing an amount.

Key Terms
كفاية (kifayah): Sufficiency, the amount that meets the wife's basic needs according to her social status and local custom, without excess or deficiency.
إدام (idam): Condiment or relish eaten with bread, such as meat, oil, milk, etc.
تقدير لازم (taqdir lazim): A fixed mandatory estimate; the Hanafi school rejects this for maintenance.

Maintenance in Hanafi fiqh is determined by sufficiency (kifaya) according to each wife's needs and local custom, not by a fixed amount.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Maintenance Standard: Husband's Status vs. Both Spouses
Analyzes the intra-Hanafi dispute on maintenance standard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on the maintenance standard and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a case of differing wealth.

Core Idea
When spouses differ in wealth, the mufta bihi imposes medium maintenance; the excess becomes a debt.

Maintenance (nafaqah) is wajib upon the husband. But what standard of living must he provide when the spouses differ in wealth? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) considers only the husband's financial status, while the later fatwa position (mufta bihi) considers both spouses and imposes a medium level. This lesson examines the evidence, the reconciliation through a debt mechanism, and the practical ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and note that there are two views: (a) Ẓāhir al-riwāya (al-Karkhī, Muḥammad): consider only the husband's status. (b) Muftā bihī (al-Khāṣṣāf, author): consider both parties and impose medium maintenance.
The mufta bihi is the operative ruling for fatwa today.
2
Examine the evidence for each position
The ẓāhir al-riwāya relies on Quran 65:7 (spend according to capacity). The mufta bihi relies on the hadith of Hind, where the Prophet considered both parties. The verse is reconciled by treating the excess as a debt.
Both proofs are accepted; the difference is in reconciliation.
3
Understand the reconciliation (debt mechanism)
The mufta bihi view holds that the husband must provide medium maintenance. If he cannot afford it, the remainder becomes a debt on him, to be paid when he has means. This preserves the verse's command while implementing the hadith.
This is the key to harmonizing the two proofs.
4
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is medium maintenance (wasat) in both scenarios: husband wealthy/wife poor and husband poor/wife wealthy. The medium is above the poor standard and below the wealthy standard.
Example: if husband eats sweets and grilled meat, he feeds his poor wife wheat bread and one or two pastries.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a poor husband married to a wealthy wife. Under ẓāhir al-riwāya, he provides poor maintenance. Under mufta bihi, he provides medium maintenance; the difference becomes a debt.
This shows the practical consequence of the dispute (thamarah al-khilaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما باعتبار حاله أو باعتبار حالهما، واختار المصنف الثاني وهو قول الخصاف، وفي الهداية وعليه الفتوى، وفي الولوالجية وهو الصحيح وعليه الفتوى وظاهر الرواية اعتبار حاله فقط وهو قول الكرخي وبه قال جمع كثير من المشايخ ونص عليه محمد وقال في التحفة والبدائع : إنه الصحيح نظراً إلى قوله تعالى لينفق ذو سعة من سعته ومن قدر عليه رزقه فلينفق مما آتاه الله لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا ما أتاها [الطلاق ] واستدل في الهداية لاعتبار حالهما بحديث هند فإنه اعتبر حالهما، وأما النص فنقول بموجبه إنه مخاطب بقدر وسعه والباقي دين في ذمته. وحاصله أنه عمل بالآية والحديث واتفقوا على وجوب نفقة الموسرين إذا كانا موسرين، وعلى نفقة المعسرين إذا كانا معسرين وإنما الاختلاف فيما إذا كان أحدهما موسراً والآخر معسراً فعلى ظاهر الرواية الاعتبار لحال الرجل ؛ فإن كان موسراً وهي معسرة تجب عليه نفقة الموسرين ولا يجب عليه أن يطعمها مما يأكل لكن قال مشايخنا : يستحب له أن يؤاكلها لأنه مأمور بحسن العشرة معها وذا في أن يؤاكلها لتكون نفقتها ونفقته سواء، وإن كان معسراً وهي موسرة وجب عليه نفقة المعسرين لأنها لما تزوجت معسراً فقد رضيت بنفقة المعسرين، وأما على المفتى به فتجب نفقة الوسط في المسألتين وهي فوق نفقة المعسرة ودون نفقة الموسرة، فإذا كان الزوج مفرطا في اليسار يأكل الحلواء واللحم المشوي والباجات والمرأة فقيرة تأكل في بيتها خبز الشعير لا يجب عليه أن يطعمها مما يأكل في بيته بنفسه ولا ما كانت تأكل في بيت أهلها ولكن يطعمها الوسط وهو خبز البر وباجة أو باجتين؛ كذا في الذخيرة . وفي غاية البيان إنه إذا كان معسراً وهي موسرة وأوجبنا الوسط فقد كلفناه بما ليس في وسعه فلا يجوز وهو غفلة عما في الهداية كما قدمناه من أنه مخاطب بقدر وسعه والباقي دين إلى الميسرة فليس تكليفاً بما ليس في وسعه. وفي المجتبى: إن شاء فرض لها أصنافاً وإن شاء قومها وفرض لها بالقيمة
As for considering his status or both of their statuses: the author chose the second, which is the statement of al-Khassaf. In al-Hidaya, it is the relied-upon fatwa. In al-Walwalijiyya, it is the correct view and the fatwa is upon it. The apparent of the riwaya is to consider only his status, which is the statement of al-Karkhi, and a large group of the mashayikh said so, and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) stated it. In al-Tuhfa and al-Bada'i, it is said that it is the correct view in light of His saying (Let the wealthy man spend according to his means, and the straitened man according to his means... [al-Talaq]). In al-Hidaya, it is argued for considering both of their statuses by the hadith of Hind, because he considered both of their statuses. As for the verse, we accept it: he is addressed according to his capacity, and the remainder is a debt on his liability. The summary is that they acted upon the verse and the hadith. They agreed on the obligation of the maintenance of the wealthy if both are wealthy, and the maintenance of the poor if both are poor. The difference is when one is wealthy and the other is poor. According to the apparent riwaya, consideration is for the man's status: if he is wealthy and she is poor, the maintenance of the wealthy is obligatory upon him, and he is not required to feed her from what he eats, but our mashayikh said: it is recommended for him to eat with her because he is commanded to treat her kindly. And if he is poor and she is wealthy, the maintenance of the poor is obligatory upon him because when she married a poor man, she agreed to the maintenance of the poor. As for the mufta bihi, the medium maintenance is obligatory in both cases, which is above the maintenance of the poor and below the maintenance of the wealthy. So if the husband is extremely wealthy, eating sweets, grilled meat, and pastries, and the woman is poor, eating barley bread in her house, he is not required to feed her what he eats in his house nor what she used to eat in her family's house, but he feeds her the medium: wheat bread and one or two pastries. This is in al-Dhakhira. In Ghayat al-Bayan, it is said: if he is poor and she is wealthy and we oblige the medium, we have burdened him beyond his capacity, which is not permissible. This is negligence of what is in al-Hidaya as we mentioned: he is addressed according to his capacity, and the remainder is a debt until ease. So it is not burdening beyond his capacity. In al-Mujtaba: if he wishes, he may impose items, and if he wishes, he may evaluate them and impose the value.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whose financial status determines the standard of maintenance when the couple differ in wealth. There are two main views: (1) Consider only the husband's status (zahir al-riwaya, supported by al-Karkhi, Muhammad, and many mashayikh), and (2) Consider both parties (al-Khassaf, the author's choice, and the mufta bihi). The relied-upon position is the second, but with a twist: the judge imposes a medium level of maintenance (above the poor, below the wealthy). The text reconciles the Quranic verse (surah al-Talaq 65:7) and the hadith of Hind. The verse requires spending according to capacity, but the hadith shows the Prophet considered Hind's situation (she was wealthy) while Abu Sufyan was poor? Actually the hadith of Hind is used to show consideration of both parties. The mufta bihi view maintains that if the husband cannot afford the medium, the remainder becomes a debt.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent/official transmission in the six core books of Imam Muhammad, considered the primary authority.
المفتى به (al-mufta bihi): The relied-upon position for issuing fatwa, which here is considering both parties and imposing medium maintenance.
وسط (wasat): The medium level of maintenance, between the rich and poor standards.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what level of maintenance is imposed when the husband is extremely wealthy and the wife is poor?
A) The maintenance of the wealthy
B) The maintenance of the poor
C) A medium level between the two
D) No maintenance until the wife complains

Key Takeaway
When spouses differ in wealth, the Hanafi mufta bihi imposes medium maintenance; any shortfall becomes a debt on the husband.

Time of Payment of Maintenance: Daily vs Monthly
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the timing of maintenance payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi views on the timing of maintenance payment and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Maintenance timing is not fixed but based on what is easiest; monthly payment is the established practice.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: should a husband pay maintenance for his wife daily or monthly? The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq reveals that the preferred view is monthly, but the principle allows flexibility based on custom. Understanding this dispute helps apply the shari‘a principle of considering benefit and ease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Read the passage to recognize that the author (al-Kanz al-Daqa'iq) did not specify the timing. This opens the door for differing views: daily, monthly, or based on circumstances.
The dispute arises from the lack of explicit text in the Qur'an and Sunnah about the frequency of payment.
2
Apply the criterion: aslah wa aysar
The passage states that the judge should impose what is most beneficial and easiest. This is the guiding principle for choosing between daily and monthly payment.
This criterion is not specific to timing but a general legal maxim in maintenance cases.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi opinions
Identify the different positions: (a) daily for wage earners, (b) monthly as reported by Imam Muhammad and adopted in al-Ikhtiyar and al-Multaqa, (c) consideration of circumstances per later scholars.
The text mentions Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi's comment that Muhammad's monthly estimate is based on custom, not a fixed rule.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The later relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is monthly imposition, as stated in al-Ikhtiyar and al-Multaqa. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Even though daily payment may be valid, the standard practice is monthly for ease and uniformity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف في أي وقت يدفع لها النفقة لأنه يختلف باختلاف الناس قالوا: يعتبر في الفرض الأصلح والأيسر؛ ففي المحترف يوماً يوما بيوم أي عليه معسر قوله : (قالوا يعتبر في الفرض الأصلح والأيسر (الخ أقول : الذي مشى عليه في الاختيار والملتقى وغيرهما التقدير بشهر بلا تفصيل. وذكر في الذخيرة أنه ذكره محمد ثم قال وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي في شرحه ما ذكره محمد: إن ما ذكره محمد من أن النفقة تفرض لها شهراً فشهراً ليس بتقدير لازم وإنما ذلك بناء على عادتهم، وبعض المتأخرين من مشايخنا قالوا يعتبر في ذلك حال
The author did not mention at what time the maintenance is paid to her, because it varies with different people. They said: in imposing it, what is most beneficial and easiest is considered; so for the wage earner, day by day, meaning daily. ... The editor said: what is consistently followed in al-Ikhtiyar, al-Multaqa, and others is the imposition by month without detail. It is mentioned in al-Dhakhira that Muhammad mentioned it, then he said: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said in his commentary on what Muhammad mentioned: what Muhammad mentioned about maintenance being imposed for her month by month is not a necessary fixed estimate, but rather it is based on their custom. Some of the later mashayikh said: in that, consideration is for...
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
The author of the original matn did not specify the timing of maintenance payments because it depends on people's circumstances. The general principle is to choose the easiest and most beneficial method for the parties. For a daily wage earner, day by day payment may be appropriate, while for others, monthly payment may be better. However, the predominant later practice, as in al-Ikhtiyar and al-Multaqa, is to impose maintenance on a monthly basis. Imam Muhammad's opinion that it should be month by month is not a fixed rule but based on the custom of his time. Some later scholars suggest considering the specific situation.

Key Terms
محترف (muhtarij): A laborer or wage earner who is paid daily.
أصلح وأيسر (aslah wa aysar): Most beneficial and easiest; the criterion for the judge in choosing the timing of payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi practice (al-Ikhtiyar, al-Multaqa), what is the default timing for maintenance payment?
A) Daily
B) Monthly
C) Annually
D) As agreed by the couple

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi for maintenance payment timing is monthly, but flexibility remains for exceptional cases based on the principle of aslah wa aysar.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Timing of Maintenance: Principle of Ease and Professional Variation
Explains the Hanafi ruling on when maintenance is due: daily, monthly, or yearly depending on the husband's profession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the timing of maintenance payment and apply the principle of ease (al-aslah wa al-aysar) to determine when a husband must pay based on his profession.

Core Idea
The timing of maintenance follows the husband's profession to ease his burden: daily for laborers, monthly for merchants, yearly for farmers.

Maintenance (nafaqah) is an obligation on the husband, but when must he pay it? The Hanafi school does not prescribe a rigid schedule. Instead, it ties the timing to the husband’s profession and income cycle, making the obligation easier to fulfill. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof from the Sunnah, and an important intra-Hanafi refinement that became the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation and its flexibility
Start by recognizing that maintenance (nafaqah) is wajib, but the timing is not fixed. The Hanafi school allows variation based on what is easiest and most suitable for the husband.
2
Apply the principle of ease to three professions
Learn the mufta bihi position: a laborer who earns daily pays daily, a merchant who earns monthly pays monthly, and a farmer who earns annually pays yearly after harvest.
This is based on the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) who refined the earlier default of monthly payment.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi difference
Note that Muhammad al-Shaybani initially prescribed monthly payment as a default, but later scholars said this was not a fixed rule; it was based on custom. The profession-based approach is now the operative view.
The hadith of Hind (خذي من ماله ما يكفيك) is used as evidence for the obligation, but timing is derived from the principle of ease, not from the hadith directly.
4
Analyze the choice between husband and wife
The passage from al-Tajnis clarifies that the husband has the choice to set the timing based on his ease, but if the wife agrees to a different schedule (e.g., daily instead of monthly), it is permissible and may be even easier for the husband.
The choice is ultimately for the husband, but the wife’s consent is respected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجتبى: إن شاء فرض لها أصنافاً وإن شاء قومها وفرض لها بالقيمة ولم يذكر المصنف في أي وقت يدفع لها النفقة لأنه يختلف باختلاف الناس قالوا: يعتبر في الفرض الأصلح والأيسر؛ ففي المحترف يوماً يوما بيوم أي عليه ماله بغير علمه فقال عليه السلام: خذي من ماله ما يكفيك ويكفي بنيك قوله: (قالوا يعتبر في الفرض الأصلح والأيسر (الخ أقول : الذي مشى عليه في الاختيار والملتقى وغيرهما التقدير بشهر بلا تفصيل. وذكر في الذخيرة أنه ذكره محمد ثم قال وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي في شرحه ما ذكره محمد: إن ما ذكره محمد من أن النفقة تفرض لها شهراً فشهراً ليس بتقدير لازم وإنما ذلك بناء على عادتهم، وبعض المتأخرين من مشايخنا قالوا يعتبر في ذلك حال الزوج فإن كان محترفاً الخ. لأن الأداء على الدهاقين إنما يتيسر عند إدراك الغلة في كل سنة، وعلى التاجر عند اتخاذ غلة الحوانيت وغيرها في كل شهر، وعلى المحترف بالاكتساب كل يوم ا هـ قوله : (وظاهر كلامهم الخ) هذا خلاف ظاهر ما نقلناه عن الذخيرة من أن المتيسر على الدهاقين عند إدراك الغلة في كل سنة الخ. فإنه ظاهر في أنه يمهل إلى وقت إدراك الغلة في آخر السنة ثم يدفع كل سنة آخرها كما لا يخفى ولو كان المراد التعجيل لم يكن فيه تخفيف على الدهقان بل الأخف عليه كل أو كل أسبوع فتأمل قوله : (وصرح به في التجنيس في نفقة الشهر الخ) أقول : المدعي كون الخيار للزوح وما استدل به عليه من كلام التجنيس مفيد أن الخيار لها لا له، وكون الخيار لها ينافي كونه للزوج فتأمل. لكن كلام التجنيس لا ينافي ما مر لأن المراد به أنه لو اختار نفقة كل شهر بشهره لا أكثر فالخيار له حيث كان فيه تخفيف عليه، فإذا رضيت منه بأخذ كل يوم بيومه فلها ذلك لأنه أخف عليه من الشهر تأمل
And in al-Mujtaba: If he wishes, he may prescribe types (of food) for her, and if he wishes, he may evaluate them and prescribe the value. And the author did not mention at what time he pays her the maintenance because it differs according to people. They said: In prescription, the most suitable and easiest is considered. For a laborer, day by day, i.e., 'Take from his wealth what suffices you and your children.' He said: (They said: In prescription, the most suitable and easiest is considered, etc.) I say: What is followed in al-Ikhtiyar, al-Multaqa, and others is the estimation by month without detail. In al-Dhakhirah, it is mentioned that Muhammad mentioned it, then he said: Shams al-Aimmah al-Sarakhsi said in his commentary: What Muhammad mentioned—that maintenance is prescribed for her month by month—is not a fixed estimation; rather, it is based on their custom. Some later scholars among our shaykhs said: In that, the husband's situation is considered: if he is a laborer, etc., because payment for farmers is only easy at the time of harvest each year, for merchants when they take the profit of shops and other things each month, and for a laborer by earning each day, end quote. He said: (And the apparent meaning of their statements, etc.) This contradicts the apparent meaning of what we transmitted from al-Dhakhirah that 'what is easy for farmers is at the time of harvest each year, etc.' For it is apparent that he is deferred until the time of harvest at the end of the year, then he pays each year at its end, as is clear. If the intent was prompt payment, there would be no ease for the farmer; rather, the easier for him is each or each week, so contemplate. He said: (And he explicitly mentioned in al-Tajnis concerning the maintenance of the month, etc.) I say: The claimant that the choice is for the husband, and what he used as evidence from the words of al-Tajnis indicates that the choice is for her, not for him, and the choice being for her contradicts it being for the husband, so contemplate. But the words of al-Tajnis do not contradict what has passed because the intended meaning is that if he chooses the maintenance of each month as a month, no more, the choice is for him where there is ease for him. So if she consents to take each day by its day, that is permissible for her because it is easier for him than the month, contemplate.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage discusses the timing of maintenance payment in the Hanafi school. The key principle is that the method and timing should be the easiest and most suitable for the husband, based on his profession. The author quotes al-Mujtaba stating that the husband can choose to give food or its value, and that the timing varies. Some scholars, like Muhammad al-Shaybani, said maintenance is prescribed monthly by default. However, later scholars tie the timing to the husband's occupation: a laborer (daily wage earner) pays daily, a merchant pays monthly, and a farmer pays yearly at harvest. This is based on ease (takhfif). The comments from al-Tajnis indicate that the choice of timing rests with the husband, but if the wife agrees to daily payment, it is permissible as it may be easier for the husband. The passage also includes a critique of a possible misunderstanding about the farmer's payment timing, clarifying that payment is made at the end of the year after harvest, not earlier.

Key Terms
تخفيف (takhfif): Easing the burden on the husband by aligning payment timing with his income schedule.
الأصلح والأيسر (al-aslah wa al-aysar): The most suitable and easiest; the criterion for timing of maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi, what is the ruling on the timing of maintenance for a merchant?
A) Daily
B) Weekly
C) Monthly
D) Yearly

Key Takeaway
The timing of maintenance in the Hanafi school is not fixed; it is determined by the husband's profession to ease his burden, with daily, monthly, or yearly schedules as the mufta bihi.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Timing of Maintenance Payment: Based on Husband's Means
Explains timing of nafaqah based on husband's profession and his option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default payment intervals for different professions and apply the principle of takhfīf to determine when a husband may choose a shorter interval.

Core Idea
Maintenance due dates align with the husband's income cycle, but he may always choose daily payment as a concession.

The obligation of nafaqah is clear, but its practical delivery raises the question of timing. Should a husband pay daily, monthly, or yearly? The Hanafi school ties the default interval to the husband's means of earning, but grants him the option to pay daily—a concession to ease the burden. This passage explores the interplay between default rules and the husband's choice, including a scholarly discussion on whether payment is in advance or at the end of the period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default payment intervals
List the professions mentioned and their corresponding payment cycles: daily for laborers, monthly for merchants, yearly for landowners, weekly for artisans. Understand that these are defaults tied to when income is typically received.
The default intervals are not arbitrary; they reflect the husband's ease of payment (takhfīf).
2
Understand the husband's option to pay daily
Note that the husband may choose to pay daily regardless of his profession, because daily payment is the easiest (takhfīf). If he opts for a longer interval, his choice is respected, but the wife may still demand her daily share if that interval is chosen.
The passage explicitly states: 'if the husband says, I will pay daily in advance, he is not forced to do otherwise' – the default is not mandatory.
3
Apply the principle of takhfīf
Recognize that the entire ruling is built on the concept of easing the husband's burden. The default intervals are a concession; the daily option is an even greater concession. Therefore, the husband's preference governs when it does not harm her.
Takhfīf is a key Hanafi principle in financial obligations.
4
Analyze the wife's right to demand daily share
If the husband chooses a longer interval (e.g., monthly), the wife may demand her share daily because the daily amount is known and she can manage her needs. This is supported by al-Tajnīs.
This protects the wife from hardship if the husband delays payment until the end of the period.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi discussion on advance vs. end-of-period payment
The passage notes a contradiction with al-Dhakhīra regarding whether landowners pay at the end of the year or in advance. The resolution is that the husband's choice determines: if he chooses yearly, he pays at the end; if he chooses daily, he pays in advance daily.
This shows the flexibility within the Hanafi school on the timing mechanism.

أن نفقة يدفع يوم بيوم لأنه قد لا يقدر على تحصيل نفقة شهر مثلاً دفعة، وهذا بناء على أن يعطيها معجلاً ويعطيها كل يوم عند المساء عن اليوم الذي يلي ذلك المساء لتتمكن من الصرف في حاجتها في ذلك اليوم، وإن كان تاجراً يفرض عليه نفقة شهر بشهر، أو من الدهاقين فنفقة سنة بسنة، أو من الصناع الذي لا ينقضي عملهم إلا بانقضاء الأسبوع كذلك؛ كذا في فتح القدير وغيره. وينبغي أن يكون محله ما إذا رضي الزوج وإلا لو قال التاجر الدهقان أو الصانع أنا أدفع نفقة كل يوم معجلاً لا يجبر على غيره لأنه إنما اعتبر ما ذكر تخفيفاً عليه فإذا كان يضره لا يفعل. وظاهر كلامهم أن كل مدة ناسبت حال الزوج فإنه يعجل نفقتها كما صرحوا به في اليوم. وصرح به في التجنيس في نفقة الشهر أنها تفرض عليه وتدفع لها ثم قال: لو فرض لها نفقة كل شهر فطلبتها كل يوم كان لها أن تطلب عند المساء لأن حصة كل يوم معلوم فيمكنها المطالبة ولا كذلك ما دون اليوم ا هـ . الغلة في شهر الزوج فإن كان محترفاً الخ. لأن الأداء على الدهاقين إنما يتيسر عند إدراك الغلة في كل سنة، وعلى التاجر عند اتخاذ غلة الحوانيت وغيرها في كل شهر، وعلى المحترف بالاكتساب كل يوم ا هـ قوله : (وظاهر كلامهم الخ) هذا خلاف ظاهر ما نقلناه عن الذخيرة من أن المتيسر على الدهاقين عند إدراك في كل سنة الخ. فإنه ظاهر في أنه يمهل إلى وقت إدراك الغلة في آخر السنة ثم يدفع كل سنة آخرها كما لا يخفى ولو كان المراد التعجيل لم يكن فيه تخفيف على الدهقان بل الأخف عليه كل أو كل أسبوع فتأمل قوله : (وصرح به في التجنيس في نفقة الشهر الخ) أقول : المدعي كون الخيار للزوح وما استدل به عليه من كلام التجنيس مفيد أن الخيار لها لا له، وكون الخيار لها ينافي كونه للزوج فتأمل. لكن كلام التجنيس لا ينافي ما مر لأن المراد به أنه لو اختار نفقة كل شهر بشهره لا أكثر فالخيار له حيث كان فيه تخفيف عليه، فإذا رضيت منه بأخذ كل يوم بيومه فلها ذلك لأنه أخف عليه من الشهر تأمل
That maintenance is paid day by day because he may not be able to afford a month's maintenance at once. This is based on giving it to her in advance, and he gives it every day at evening for the following day so that she can spend on her needs that day. If he is a merchant, maintenance is imposed on him month by month; if from landowners, then yearly by year; if from artisans whose work is only completed at the end of the week, similarly. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and others. And it should be conditioned on the husband's consent; otherwise, if the merchant, landowner, or artisan says 'I will pay maintenance every day in advance', he is not forced to do otherwise, because what was mentioned is considered as a concession for him. If it harms him, he does not do it. And the apparent meaning of their words is that every period suitable to the husband's condition, he gives her maintenance in advance as they explicitly stated for the day. And it is explicitly stated in al-Tajnīs regarding monthly maintenance that it is imposed on him and paid to her. Then he said: If he imposed monthly maintenance and she demands it daily, she may demand it at evening, because the share of each day is known and she can demand it, unlike less than a day. End. The yield (ghalla) in the month of the husband, if he is a professional, etc., because payment for landowners is only feasible when the yield is realized yearly; for the merchant, when the profit from shops etc. is realized monthly; for the professional, by earning daily. End. His statement: (And the apparent meaning of their words etc.) This contradicts what we transmitted from al-Dhakhīra that what is feasible for landowners is when the yield is realized yearly etc. For it is apparent that he is given respite until the time of realizing the yield at the end of the year, then he pays at the end of each year, as is not hidden. If the intent were advance payment, there would be no concession for the landowner; rather, the easier for him would be every day or every week. Reflect. His statement: (And he explicitly stated in al-Tajnīs regarding monthly maintenance etc.) I say: The claim that the choice is for the husband, and what he used as evidence from the words of al-Tajnīs indicates that the choice is for her, not him, and that the choice being for her contradicts it being for the husband. Reflect. But the words of al-Tajnīs do not contradict what passed, because what is meant is that if he chooses monthly maintenance month by month and no more, the choice is for him where it is a concession for him. If she agrees to take it day by day, she has that because it is easier for him than monthly. Reflect
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the timing of maintenance payment. The standard rule is that maintenance is paid in installments corresponding to the husband's income cycle: daily for laborers, monthly for merchants, yearly for landowners, weekly for artisans. However, the husband has the option to pay daily if that is easier for him. The commentary from Fath al-Qadir and al-Tajnīs clarifies that if the husband opts for a longer period (e.g., monthly), the wife may still demand her daily share because the daily amount is known. The text also discusses a marginal dispute from al-Dhakhīra about whether the landowner pays at the end of the year or in advance, with the conclusion that advance payment is not required if it burdens him. The key principle is ease (takhfīf) for the husband.

Key Terms
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance that a husband must provide for his wife, including food, clothing, and shelter.
تخفيف (takhfīf): Concession or easing of the burden on the husband, justifying the option to pay daily.
دهقان (dahqān): Landowner or farmer who receives income from land yields, typically once a year.
تاجر (tājir): Merchant whose income from trade is realized monthly.
محترف (muhtarif): Professional laborer or craftsman who earns daily wages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) She may demand the entire month's amount in advance.
D) She may demand weekly payments instead.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance timing defaults align with the husband's income cycle, but he may always choose daily payment, and if he chooses a longer interval, his wife may demand her daily share.

Condition of In-Kind Maintenance and Finality of Judicial Rulings
Analyzes two rulings: in-kind maintenance condition and inter-madhhab judge rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a condition for in-kind maintenance is void and explain why a judge's ruling cannot be reversed by a judge of another madhhab.

Core Idea
A condition for in-kind maintenance is not binding; a valid ijtihad-based judgment is final across schools.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies two interconnected issues: the enforceability of contractual maintenance conditions and the binding nature of judicial rulings. The Hanafi school maintains that maintenance must be in cash as a default, and any stipulation to the contrary is void. Furthermore, once a judge rules according to his ijtihad, that ruling cannot be overturned by another judge of a different madhhab, even if that judge would have ruled differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Recognize the specific contractual condition: the husband stipulates that maintenance (nafaqah) will be provided in kind (tamwīn) rather than cash.
The condition is made at the time of the marriage contract.
2
State the Hanafi Position
According to the Hanafi school, such a condition is not binding (غیر لازم). The wife retains the right to demand cash maintenance as the default obligation.
This is based on the principle that maintenance is a financial duty, and cash ensures clarity and stability.
3
Examine the Proof
The Hanafi proof is based on the nature of maintenance: it arises from marriage (the first cause) and is a fixed obligation. The condition attempts to alter that nature, making it void.
Contrast with the Shafi'i view that contractual conditions should be honored.
4
Extend to Judicial Rulings
The passage then applies a similar logic to judges' rulings: once a Hanafi judge rules for cash maintenance, a Shafi'i judge cannot reverse it because the ruling rests on the first cause (marriage) and reflects valid ijtihad.
The principle is that a valid ijtihad-based judgment is final and cannot be overturned by another judge of a different madhhab.
5
Apply to Cross-Madhhab Scenarios
Conversely, if a Shafi'i judge rules for in-kind maintenance, a Hanafi judge cannot overturn that ruling either. The judgment stands, maintaining legal stability.
This prevents endless litigation and respects the authority of the first judge's ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن قلت إذا شرط عليها الخ قال في النهر : واعلم أنه لو شرط في العقد أن النفقة تموين كان الشرط غير لازم ولو حكم بموجب العقد حاكم يرى ذلك عرف ذلك من مارس كتبهم. بقي أنه لو حكم الحنفي بفرضها دراهم واستوفى ما لا بد منه هل للشافعي أن يحكم بالتموين بعد ذلك ؟ قال الشيخ قاسم في موجبات الأحكام بعد ما ذكر صورة سجل النفقة قلت: هذا دليل لما أقول من أنه ليس للشافعي ذلك بناء على أن الزوجية والقرابة سبب لوجوبها بشرطها وإن كان كل يوم سبباً لنفقته أيضاً وأن القضاء يعتمد السبب الأول وتبدل الحال والسعر ونحو ذلك يعتمد السبب الثاني ا هـ . وعلى هذا فلو حكم الشافعي بالتموين ليس للحنفي أن يحكم
His statement: (If you say: If he stipulated at the time of contract that maintenance is provisioning (tamwīn), etc. He said in al-Nahr: Know that if he stipulates in the contract that maintenance is provisioning, the condition is not binding. And if a judge who sees that rules according to the contract, that is known from those who frequent their books. It remains: if a Hanafi judge ruled to impose it in dirhams and took what is necessary, can a Shafi'i judge rule for provisioning after that? Shaykh Qāsim said in Mūjibāt al-Aḥkām after mentioning the form of the maintenance record: I say: This is evidence for what I say, that the Shafi'i cannot do that, because marriage and kinship are the cause of its obligation with its condition, even though each day is also a cause for his maintenance. And the ruling depends on the first cause, while change in circumstances and prices etc. depends on the second cause. End. Based on this, if a Shafi'i judge rules for provisioning, a Hanafi judge cannot rule (otherwise).
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues. First, if a marriage contract stipulates that maintenance is to be in kind (تموين) without cash estimation, that condition is not binding (غير لازم) in Hanafi law, because maintenance is fundamentally a financial obligation that should be payable in currency. Second, it discusses the inter-madhhab effect of a judge's ruling: if a Hanafi judge has already imposed cash maintenance, a Shafi'i judge cannot later reverse it to in-kind, because the obligation is established on the primary cause (marriage). Conversely, if a Shafi'i judge ruled for in-kind, a Hanafi judge cannot overturn it either, because the ruling is valid and respects the first cause. This is based on the principle that a valid judgment from a mujtahid judge is not to be reversed by another judge of a different madhhab, even if the latter considers it incorrect.

Key Terms
تموين (tamwīn): Provisioning maintenance in kind (food, clothing) rather than cash.
حكم الحاكم (ḥukm al-ḥākim): The ruling of a judge which is binding and cannot be reversed by another judge of a different madhhab on the same matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the Hanafi school considers in-kind invalid.
B) No, because the Shafi'i ruling is based on valid ijtihad and cannot be overturned.
C) Yes, if the wife petitions a new judge.
D) No, but only if the husband agrees.

Key Takeaway
A condition for in-kind maintenance is void in Hanafi law, and a valid judicial ruling cannot be overturned by a judge of a different madhhab.
