Discharge from Maintenance and Cross-Madhhab Judicial Effects
Ruling on valid discharge and effect of a Maliki judge's ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid discharge from maintenance and explain why a Hanafi judge can assess maintenance despite a prior Maliki ruling on the marriage contract.

Core Idea
Discharge from maintenance is only valid for past due amounts; a Maliki judge's contract ruling does not bar a Hanafi judge from assessing future maintenance.

This passage clarifies when a wife's discharge (ibrā') from her husband's maintenance obligation is legally effective. It also addresses a practical cross-madhhab scenario: if a Maliki judge has confirmed the marriage contract, can a Hanafi judge later set a maintenance amount? The answer hinges on the nature of maintenance as a recurring obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on discharge
Extract the Hanafi position: discharge is valid only for maintenance that has already become due (by court order or consent) and for a past period.
Future maintenance cannot be waived because it is not yet an established debt.
2
Understand the condition of obligation
Maintenance becomes a specific debt only after a judge's estimation or mutual agreement for a defined period. Without that, the amount is uncertain and discharge is invalid.
The text cites al-Bazzāziyya: discharge of the first month is valid if the judge set a monthly amount, but not subsequent months.
3
Analyze the cross-madhhab scenario
A Maliki judge's ruling on the marriage contract does not constitute a ruling on maintenance. Maintenance is renewed daily, so no prior judgment covers future periods.
The author argues from Fuṣūl al-ʿImādī and al-Qunyah that a valid judgment requires a claim and an event; maintenance is ongoing.
4
Apply the principle to the Hanafi judge
The Hanafi judge can assess maintenance for the wife because the Maliki ruling did not address the specific maintenance claim, and the obligation is new each day.
The judge's estimation is itself a ruling (taqdīr), akin to imprisoning a defendant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ هو شرط فيما لم يكن واجباً بعد ولهذا قالوا: إن الإبراء عن النفقة لا يصح إلا إذا وجبت بالقضاء أو الرضا ومضت مدة فحينئذ يصح الإبراء ؛ كذا في البدائع. وفي البزازية : أنت بريء من نفقتي ما دمت امرأتك فإن لم يفرض القاضي النفقة فالإبراء باطل، وإن فرض لها القاضي النفقة كل شهر عشرة دراهم صح الإبراء من نفقة الشهر الأول دون ما سواها ا هـ . وهذا يدل عل أن التقدير في مثل هذا يقع على الشهر الأول دون ما عداه. فإن قلت: إذا حكم مالكي في أصل العقد وفي شروطه وكتب وحكم بموجبه كما يفعل الآن ثم بعد ذلك شكت المرأة وطلبت التقرير عند قاض حنفي فهل له تقريرها؟ قلت: لم أره صريحاً أيضاً وما نقلوه في كتاب القضاء كما في فصول العمادي والبزازية من أن الحكم لا يرفع الخلاف إلا إذا كان بعد دعوى صحيحة في حادثة من خصم على خصم وما نقل الكل من أن شرط صحة الحكم تقدم الدعوى والحادثة يقتضي أن للحنفي ذلك وقد كثر وقوعها في زماننا خصوصاً أن النفقة تتجدد في كل يوم وما يتجدد لم يقع فيه حكم. وفي القنية : قول القاضي استديني عليه كل شهر كذا فرض منه كحبس المدعى عليه قضاء به .
Because it is a condition for what is not yet obligatory; therefore they said: Discharge from maintenance is not valid unless it becomes obligatory by court order or consent and a period has passed. Then discharge is valid. Thus in al-Badāʾiʿ. And in al-Bazzāziyya: 'You are free from my maintenance as long as you are my wife' — if the judge has not imposed maintenance, the discharge is invalid. If the judge imposed maintenance of ten dirhams per month, discharge from the first month's maintenance is valid, but not beyond that. End. This indicates that estimation in such matters applies to the first month only. If you say: If a Maliki judge has ruled on the original contract and its conditions and written and ruled accordingly, as is done now, then afterwards the woman complains and requests assessment from a Hanafi judge, can he assess? I say: I have not seen it explicitly either. And what they transmitted in the book of Judgments, as in Fuṣūl al-ʿImādī and al-Bazzāziyya, that judgment does not remove disagreement unless after a valid claim in an event from one litigant against another, and what all transmitted that the condition for a valid judgment is prior claim and event, necessitates that the Hanafi has that (right). And it has become frequent in our time, especially since maintenance is renewed daily, and what is renewed has not been subject to a judgment. And in al-Qunyah: The judge's statement 'Incur debt on him for each month, such and such' is considered a ruling from him, like imprisoning the defendant, it is a ruling thereby.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage first addresses the validity of a wife's discharge (ibraʾ) from maintenance. Discharge is only valid after maintenance has become due, either by a judge's order or by mutual consent, and only for past periods. For future maintenance, discharge is invalid because it is not yet owed. The example from al-Bazzāziyya clarifies: if a judge orders ten dirhams per month, discharge of the first month is valid but not subsequent months. This shows that the judge's estimation applies only to the first month. The second part discusses a scenario where a Maliki judge has ruled on the marriage contract and its conditions. The wife then seeks a maintenance assessment from a Hanafi judge. The author concludes that the Hanafi judge can assess because the Maliki ruling did not involve a specific maintenance claim, and maintenance is renewed daily, so no prior judgment exists on the daily liabilities. The Hanafi judge's assessment is considered a new ruling for future periods.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibrāʾ): Discharge or release from debt; here, wife declaring her husband free from maintenance obligation.
تقدير القاضي (taqdīr al-qāḍī): The judge's estimation of the amount of maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Discharge from maintenance is valid only for past due amounts; a prior Maliki ruling on the marriage does not prevent a Hanafi judge from assessing future maintenance.

Items Included in Maintenance: Firewood, Soap, Water, and Midwife
Lists specific items covered by maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which household expenses are part of the husband's maintenance obligation and identify the exceptions regarding water for wudū and menstrual purification.

Core Idea
Maintenance includes firewood, soap, oil, and ghusl water; but wudū water is always on the wife.

We have learned that the husband must provide maintenance (nafaqah) for his wife, but what exactly does that include? This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq lists specific items that are part of that obligation, as well as key exceptions. Understanding these details is crucial for both spouses to know their rights and duties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for wife's recourse
The passage begins by stating that if the husband fails to provide maintenance or clothing, the wife may lawfully take from his food and fabric without permission. This is a legal remedy, not theft.
This is based on the husband's obligation to provide; his failure gives her this right.
2
List the main items included in maintenance
The author explicitly lists: firewood (for heating and cooking), soap, potash (a cleaning agent), oil (for lamps and other uses), and the price of water for bathing (ghusl). These are all on the husband.
The basis for ghusl water is that it is 'muʾnat al-jimāʿ' (expense of intercourse), which benefits the husband.
3
Note the exception and dispute regarding water for purification from menses
According to the book of Razīn, the cost of water for purification from menses depends on the duration: if her menses last exactly ten days (full cycle), it is on her; if less, it is on the husband. This is a detail from a non-standard source.
This shows that rulings can vary based on specifics.
4
Understand the midwife fee ruling
If a midwife is hired by the wife or husband, the fee is on the hirer. If she comes without being hired, there are two opinions: one considers it part of maintenance (on husband) because it relates to childbirth, which is owed to him; the other considers it on the wife, like a doctor's fee.
This is a legitimate ikhtilāf; both are valid views.
5
Remember the fixed exception: water for wudūʾ
The passage concludes: the price of water for wudūʾ (ablution for prayer) is always on the wife. Even if she is wealthy, she must hire someone to bring it rather than carry it herself (to maintain dignity).
This is not part of maintenance because it is purely her own worship duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف النفقة عليه إلى أنه إذا لم يعط الزوج لها نفقة ولا كسوة فلها أن بوجوب تنفق من طعامه وتتخذ ثوباً من كرباسه بغير إذنه كما في الذخيرة والقنية . ومن النفقة التي على الزوج الحطب والصابون والأشنان والدهن للاستصباح وغيره وثمن ماء الاغتسال لأنه مؤنة الجماع. وفي كتاب رزين جعله عليها وفصل في ماء الطهر من الحيض بين أن يكون حيضها عشرة أيام فعليها أو أقل فعليه، وأجرة القابلة على من استأجرها من الزوجة والزوج، فإن جاءت بغير استئجار فلقائل أن يقول عليه لأنه مؤنة الجماع ولقائل أن يقول عليها كأجرة الطبيب وأما ثمن ماء الوضوء فعليها فإن كانت غنية تستأجر من ينقله ولا تنقله
And the author pointed out that if the husband does not give her maintenance or clothing, she may rightfully spend from his food and take a garment from his fabric without his permission, as in al-Dhakhīra and al-Qunyah. And from the maintenance that is upon the husband: firewood, soap, potash, oil for lighting and other uses, and the price of water for bathing (ghusl) because it is for the purpose of intercourse. And in the book of Razīn, he made it upon her, and he differentiated regarding water for purification from menstruation: if her menses are ten days, it is upon her; if less, upon him. And the fee of the midwife: if she is hired by the wife or husband, [the fee is on the hirer]. If she comes without hiring, one may say it is upon him because it is for intercourse, and one may say it is upon her like the doctor's fee. As for the price of water for wuḍūʾ, it is upon her. If she is wealthy, she hires someone to bring it and does not carry it herself.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage enumerates various items that are included in the husband's maintenance obligation. If the husband fails to provide maintenance, the wife may take from his food and clothing without permission (a valid legal recourse). Specific items: firewood for cooking/heating, soap, potash (a cleaning agent), oil for lamps, and water for bathing (ghusl) because it facilitates marital relations. There is a difference regarding water for purification from menstruation: if her menses last ten days (full cycle), the cost is on her; if less, on him. Midwife fees: depends on who hires her; if she comes without hiring, there are two opinions. Water for wudūʾ (ablution) is always on the wife. The passage also notes that if the wife is wealthy, she must hire someone to bring water for wudūʾ and not carry it herself (to preserve dignity).

Key Terms
مؤنة الجماع (muʾnat al-jimāʿ): Expenses related to marital intercourse, such as water for bath.
القابلة (al-qābila): Midwife; her fee is part of maintenance if she assists in childbirth.
الأشنان (al-ushnān): Potash, a cleaning agent used like soap.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who bears the cost of water for wudūʾ (ablution)?
A) The husband
B) The wife
C) The husband if she is wealthy
D) Shared equally

Key Takeaway
Maintenance in Hanafi fiqh includes many household necessities; water for ghusl is on the husband, but water for wudū is always on the wife.

intermediate
6 min
Judge's Estimation of Maintenance: A Binding Judgment for Future Periods
Confirms that a judge's estimation of maintenance is a binding judgment for future periods until a change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a judge's estimation of maintenance (nafaqah) is a binding judicial ruling (ḥukm) that makes future maintenance wajib, and apply the rule concerning discharge (ibrāʾ) after such estimation.

Core Idea
A judge's maintenance order binds the husband for future periods, even after the period ends, until a valid impediment arises.

This lesson examines a key principle from Hanafi fiqh: the judge's estimation of spousal maintenance is not merely an assessment but a binding judgment. The passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq clarifies how such an order makes future maintenance obligatory, and how the wife's discharge (ibrāʾ) operates after the judge's ruling. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly applying nafaqah law in court situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine the main claim: that the judge's estimation of maintenance is a binding ruling (ḥukm). Note the author's direct answer: 'It is a ruling.'
2
Examine the evidence from Khizānat al-Muftīn
The author cites a text from Nafaqāt Khizānat al-Muftīn that describes the judge's pronouncement: 'I have imposed upon you maintenance...' This makes the maintenance wajib even after the period passes.
This demonstrates the legal effect: future maintenance becomes wajib by the ruling.
3
Analyze the effect of discharge (ibrāʾ)
The source explains that if the wife discharges the husband after the judge's imposition, it is valid for the period covered. Example: monthly maintenance of ten dirhams; discharge of the first month is valid. After a month passes, discharge of that month and one future month is valid.
Discharge is valid only for periods that have become due or have been specified by the ruling.
4
Understand the scope of the ruling
The ruling covers all future periods as long as the marriage continues, unless an impediment (māniʿ) arises. The judge's wording (e.g., 'as long as she is in wedlock') extends the obligation.
The binding nature continues until a change in circumstances.
5
Connect to the broader fiqh principle
The author notes that this topic will be revisited under the statement 'And maintenance that has passed is not obligatory except by court order or consent.' This highlights the general rule: past maintenance not due without a ruling or agreement.
This lesson focuses on the forward-looking effect of the judge's order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البحر من القضاء فإن قلت هل تقدير القاضي النفقة حكم منه؟ قلت: هو حكم وطلب التقدير بشرطه دعوى فقد وجد بعد الدعوى والحادثة، ويدل عليه ما في نفقات خزانة المفتين : وإذا أراد القاضي أن يفرض النفقة يقول فرضت عليك نفقة لا تسقط امرأتك كذا وكذا أو يقول فرضت عليك النفقة مدة كذا يصح وتجب على الزوج حتى بمضي المدة لأن نفقة الزمان المستقبل تصير واجبة بقضاء القاضي حتى لو أبرأت بعد الفرض صح . فإن قلت : إذا فرض لها كل يوم أو كل شهر هل يكون قضاء بالجميع ما دامت في العصمة؟ قلت : نعم ما لم يمنع مانع بدليل ما في الخزانة : فرض كل شهر عشرة فأبرأته من نفقتها برىء من الشهر الأول ، فإذا مضى شهر فأبرأته من نفقة ما مضى وما يستقبل برىء مما مضى ومن شهر مما يستقبل وتمامه فيها اهـ. قلت سيأتي هذا عند قول المصنف ولا تجب نفقة مضت إلا بالقضاء أو الرضا» .
And in al-Baḥr from the section of Judgments: If you say: Is the judge's estimation of maintenance a ruling from him? I say: It is a ruling, and the request for estimation is a claim; it occurs after the claim and the event. Evidence for it is what is in Nafaqāt Khizānat al-Muftīn: When the judge wants to impose maintenance, he says: 'I have imposed upon you maintenance for your wife, not less than such and such' or says: 'I have imposed upon you maintenance for such a period.' It is valid and becomes obligatory on the husband even after the period passes, because future maintenance becomes wajib by the judge's ruling. So even if she discharges him after the imposition, it is valid. If you say: If he imposed for her daily or monthly, is it a ruling for all as long as she is in wedlock? I say: Yes, unless there is an impediment. Evidence is what is in al-Khizāna: He imposed ten per month, and she discharged him from her maintenance; he is free from the first month. Then when a month passed, she discharged him from past and future; he is free from what passed and from one month of future. The rest is in it. End. I say: This will come at the statement of the author: 'And maintenance that has passed is not obligatory except by court order or consent.'
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage clarifies that a judge's estimation of maintenance is a binding judicial ruling (ḥukm). The request for estimation is a valid claim. The judge's statement imposing maintenance (e.g., 'I impose on you maintenance for your wife of x amount' or 'for such period') makes future maintenance due, even for periods after the ruling. Consequently, the wife's discharge (ibraʾ) after such a ruling is valid for the period specified. If the judge says 'ten per month,' discharge of the first month is valid; after a month passes, discharge of that month and one future month is valid. The ruling covers all future periods as long as the marriage continues, unless an impediment arises. The author notes that this topic will be revisited later under the author's statement about maintenance not being due without court order or consent.

Key Terms
تقدير القاضي (taqdīr al-qāḍī): The judge's assessment and imposition of maintenance amount and period.
الإبراء بعد الفرض (al-ibrāʾ baʿda al-farḍ): Discharge from maintenance after the judge's estimation; valid for the period covered by the estimation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a judge imposes monthly maintenance of ten dirhams and the wife immediately discharges her husband?
A) The discharge is valid for all future months
B) The discharge is valid only for the first month
C) The discharge is invalid because maintenance is not yet due
D) The discharge is valid only if the husband agrees

Key Takeaway
A judge's estimation of maintenance is a binding judgment that makes future maintenance wajib; discharge after the ruling is valid only for the period that has already become due or is specified.

Waiver of Maintenance Only Valid After Obligation
Ruling on conditions for valid waiver of maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid waiver of maintenance (ibrā' al-nafaqah) and determine validity in a given scenario.

Core Idea
A waiver of maintenance is valid only after the obligation has accrued; future maintenance cannot be waived.

In Hanafi law, the right to maintenance becomes an actual debt only when it falls due. The wife cannot waive future maintenance because it is not yet owed. This lesson examines the precise conditions—court assessment or mutual agreement, and the passage of time—that make a waiver legally effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is ibrā' (waiver) of nafaqah (maintenance). Determine the general ruling: valid only after obligation has accrued.
2
Locate the condition for validity
Maintenance must have become obligatory by court order (qadā') or mutual consent (riḍā), and a period must have passed so that maintenance for that period is due.
Without these, the obligation is not yet established.
3
Distinguish present vs future
Only maintenance that has already accrued can be waived. Future maintenance is not yet owed and therefore cannot be waived
The text explicitly uses the example of waiving the first month after it is due.
4
Apply to sample statements
A general statement like 'you are free from my maintenance as long as you are my wife' is void if no specific obligation exists. If the judge sets a monthly amount, waiving the first month (past) is valid, but waiving future months is not.
This illustrates the principle in practice.
5
Note the practical consequence
This rule prevents indefinite waivers and protects the wife's right to future support. A husband cannot pressure his wife into relinquishing rights that have not yet materialized.
The underlying rationale is safeguarding the wife's welfare.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ هو شرط فيما لم يكن واجباً بعد ولهذا قالوا: إن الإبراء عن النفقة لا يصح إلا إذا وجبت بالقضاء أو الرضا ومضت مدة فحينئذ يصح الإبراء؛ كذا في البدائع. وفي البزازية: أنت بريء من نفقتي ما دمت امرأتك فإن لم يفرض القاضي النفقة فالإبراء باطل، وإن فرض لها القاضي النفقة كل شهر عشرة دراهم صح الإبراء من نفقة الشهر الأول دون ما سواها ا هـ.
Because it is a condition in what is not yet obligatory. For this reason they said: Waiver of maintenance is not valid unless it becomes obligatory by court order or consent and a period has passed; then waiver is valid. As in al-Bada'i. And in al-Bazzaziyya: 'You are free from my maintenance as long as you are my wife' – if the judge has not assessed maintenance, the waiver is void. If the judge assessed maintenance at ten dirhams per month, waiver of the first month's maintenance is valid, but not beyond that.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about the validity of waiving (ibrā') maintenance. The key principle is that one can only waive something that is already due and owed. Future maintenance is not yet an obligation, so waiving it in advance is invalid. The text gives two examples: first, if the husband says 'you are free from my maintenance as long as you are my wife' without a court assessment, it is void because no specific obligation exists. Second, if the judge sets a monthly amount, waiving the first month's maintenance (which has passed) is valid, but waiving future months is not. This is because each month's maintenance becomes obligatory only as it falls due.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibrā'): Waiver or release from a debt or obligation; here specifically the husband releasing the wife from her right to maintenance.
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Court order; here, the judge's assessment of maintenance.
الرضا (al-riḍā): Mutual consent between husband and wife regarding the amount of maintenance.
المدة (al-muddah): A period of time that must pass for maintenance to become due and payable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a wife's waiver of maintenance valid?
A) At any time, even before marriage.
B) Only after the maintenance has become due by court order or consent and a period has passed.
C) Only if the husband agrees in writing.
D) Only if the wife is wealthy.

Key Takeaway
A waiver of maintenance is only valid for amounts that have already become due; future maintenance cannot be waived in advance.

Hanafi Judge Confirming Maliki Maintenance Ruling
Inter-madhhab issue: Hanafi judge confirming Maliki maintenance order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a Hanafi judge may assess maintenance for a wife despite a previous Maliki ruling, and identify the condition that makes a prior ruling binding.

Core Idea
A prior court ruling does not bind for recurring obligations like maintenance because each day is a new incident.

This lesson addresses a practical inter-madhhab scenario: a wife has obtained a maintenance order from a Maliki judge, but seeks confirmation from a Hanafi judge. The Hanafi jurists ruled that a previous ruling only binds if it was based on a specific incident and claim. Since maintenance is a recurring obligation, each new day constitutes a new incident, allowing a fresh assessment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage asks whether a Hanafi judge can confirm (taqrīr) maintenance after a Maliki judge's ruling.
2
Recall the general principle on court rulings
A ruling only removes disagreement (i.e., is binding) if it was made after a valid claim (da'wa) and a specific incident (haditha).
Source: Fusul al-Imadi and al-Bazzaziyya.
3
Apply the principle to maintenance
Maintenance renews daily, so each day is a new incident. The previous ruling only covers past periods.
The text says 'النفقة تتجدد في كل يوم'.
4
Consider the practical frequency
The author notes this situation is common, and the Hanafi judge may therefore assess.
5
Confirm with an authoritative source
Al-Qunyah states that the judge's order to incur debt monthly is a valid assessment and a ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: إذا حكم مالكي في أصل العقد وفي شروطه وكتب وحكم بموجبه كما يفعل الآن ثم بعد ذلك شكت المرأة وطلبت التقرير عند قاض حنفي فهل له تقريرها؟ قلت: لم أره صريحاً أيضاً وما نقلوه في كتاب القضاء كما في فصول العمادي والبزازية من أن الحكم لا يرفع الخلاف إلا إذا كان بعد دعوى صحيحة في حادثة من خصم على خصم وما نقل الكل من أن شرط صحة الحكم تقدم الدعوى والحادثة يقتضي أن للحنفي ذلك وقد كثر وقوعها في زماننا خصوصاً أن النفقة تتجدد في كل يوم وما يتجدد لم يقع فيه حكم. وفي القنية: قول القاضي استديني عليه كل شهر كذا فرض منه كحبس المدعى عليه قضاء به.
If you say: When a Maliki judge has ruled on the original marriage contract and its conditions, and written and ruled accordingly as is done now, then afterwards the woman complains and requests confirmation from a Hanafi judge, does he have the right to confirm it? I say: I have not seen it explicitly either. And what they transmitted in the book of qada', as in Fusul al-Imadi and al-Bazzaziyya, that a ruling does not remove disagreement unless it is after a valid claim in an incident from one opponent against another. And everyone transmitted that the condition for validity of a ruling is the prior existence of a claim and an incident. This necessitates that the Hanafi judge may do so, and it has become frequent in our time, especially since maintenance renews every day, and what renews has not been the subject of a ruling. And in al-Qunyah: The judge's statement 'incur debt upon him every month such and such' is a assessment from him, like imprisoning the defendant, thereby ruling.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage addresses a practical situation: a Maliki judge has already given a ruling on the marriage and associated matters. Later, the wife wants a Hanafi judge to confirm or assess maintenance. The question is whether the Hanafi judge can do so. The author notes that a previous court ruling only removes disagreement (i.e., is binding) if it was made after a valid claim (da'wa) and a specific incident. Since maintenance is a recurring obligation that renews daily, the past Maliki ruling may not cover future periods. Therefore, the Hanafi judge can assess maintenance for future periods, as each day's maintenance is a new incident. The text also cites al-Qunyah that a judge's order to the husband to 'incur debt' for monthly maintenance is a valid assessment and a ruling.

Key Terms
تقرير (taqrīr): Confirmation or assessment of maintenance by a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, can a Hanafi judge assess maintenance for a wife who already has a Maliki ruling?
A) No, because the Maliki ruling is binding.
B) Yes, but only if the Maliki judge consents.
C) Yes, because each day's maintenance is a new incident not covered by the previous ruling.
D) No, because maintenance is a single obligation.

Key Takeaway
A prior ruling does not bar a Hanafi judge from assessing maintenance for future periods because maintenance is a recurring obligation and each day is a new incident.

Wife May Take Maintenance Without Permission if Husband Fails
Ruling on wife's right to take maintenance without consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a wife taking maintenance without her husband's permission and identify the conditions that justify it.

Core Idea
A wife may take basic necessities from her husband without permission when he fails to provide.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a practical scenario: a husband neglects his duty of providing maintenance (nafaqah) and clothing (kiswa). In such a case, Islamic law grants the wife a limited right to take from his provisions directly, without seeking his consent. This lesson examines the ruling, its conditions, and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the wife taking her husband's food or cloth without his permission. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) under the stated condition.
Normally taking another's property is haram, but necessity changes the ruling.
2
Identify the condition that permits the act
The husband must have failed to give her nafaqah (maintenance) or kiswa (clothing). Without this failure, the wife has no right to take.
The failure must be actual; mere delay may not suffice without evidence of refusal.
3
Determine what she may take
She may take food (ta'am) and cloth (thawb from kirbās). This is limited to basic necessities, not cash or luxury items.
Kirbās is an example of coarse cotton cloth; the specific fabric may vary by local custom.
4
Note the source authority
The text cites al-Dhakhira and al-Qunya as sources, indicating this ruling is well-established in the Hanafi school.
These are authoritative later Hanafi works (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
5
Consider the limitations
The wife may take only what suffices for her basic needs, and only as long as the husband continues to withhold. She cannot take more than needed or after he provides.
If the husband later gives maintenance, the permission ceases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف النفقة عليه إلى أنه إذا لم يعط الزوج لها نفقة ولا كسوة فلها أن تنفق من طعامه وتتخذ ثوباً من كرباسه بغير إذنه كما في الذخيرة والقنية.
And the author indicated that if the husband does not give her maintenance or clothing, she may spend from his food and take a garment from his coarse cloth without his permission, as in al-Dhakhira and al-Qunya.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage states a ruling of necessity: when a husband fails to provide maintenance (nafaqah) or clothing (kiswa), the wife is permitted to take from his provisions without his consent. She may take food to eat and cloth to make a garment. This is a concession to prevent harm, as the husband is obligated to provide but is neglecting his duty. The sources al-Dhakhira and al-Qunya confirm this. Note that this is not a right to take cash or other property, but only basic necessities.

Key Terms
كرباس (kirbās): Coarse cotton cloth, a basic material for clothing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When may a wife take her husband's food without his permission?
A) Never, because it is theft.
B) Only if she is starving.
C) If he fails to give her maintenance or clothing.
D) Only if the judge allows it.

Key Takeaway
A wife may take basic food and clothing from her husband's provisions without his permission only if he fails to provide maintenance, based on the principle of necessity.

The Judge's Fixing of Maintenance as a Binding Verdict
Ruling on the nature of judge's maintenance fixation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the judge's assessment of maintenance is a valid judicial verdict and explain its effect on future maintenance obligations and the validity of waivers.

Core Idea
The judge's verdict on maintenance is itself a hukm, making future installments due as they arise.

In Hanafi law, when a judge sets the amount of maintenance, that act is not merely a recommendation—it is a fully binding judicial verdict (hukm). This lesson examines the conditions for this verdict, its effect on future periods, and the implications of the wife's waiver after the assessment. The source passage uses a question-and-answer format to clarify these points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger for the judge's assessment
The judge's power to assess maintenance requires two elements: a claim (da'wa) by the wife and an incident (haditha) that gives rise to the claim. Without a proper claim, the assessment is not a valid verdict.
This distinguishes it from the judge's own initiative.
2
Understand the nature of the assessment as a hukm
The passage explicitly states that the judge's fixing (taqdīr) of maintenance is a hukm (ruling). This means it creates legal obligations, not just moral guidance.
Hukm here means a binding judicial decision.
3
Analyze the effect on future maintenance
Once the judge pronounces the assessment, the husband becomes obliged to pay for future periods as they arrive. The obligation is established by the ruling itself, not by the passing of each month.
Example: if judge fixes 10 per month, the obligation for month 2 is already established, but the debt only becomes due when month 2 begins.
4
Examine the waiver scenario
If the wife waives maintenance after the judge's assessment, she can only waive amounts that have already become due (e.g., the first month). She cannot waive future months that have not yet become due.
This shows that the ruling creates a contingent obligation that materializes over time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البحر من القضاء فإن قلت هل تقدير القاضي النفقة حكم منه؟ قلت: هو حكم وطلب التقدير بشرطه دعوى فقد وجد بعد الدعوى والحادثة، ويدل عليه ما في نفقات خزانة المفتين: وإذا أراد القاضي أن يفرض النفقة يقول فرضت عليك نفقة لا تسقط امرأتك كذا وكذا أو يقول فرضت عليك النفقة مدة كذا يصح وتجب على الزوج حتى بمضي المدة لأن نفقة الزمان المستقبل تصير واجبة بقضاء القاضي حتى لو أبرأت بعد الفرض صح. فإن قلت: إذا فرض لها كل يوم أو كل شهر هل يكون قضاء بالجميع ما دامت في العصمة؟ قلت: نعم ما لم يمنع مانع بدليل ما في الخزانة: فرض كل شهر عشرة فأبرأته من نفقتها برىء من الشهر الأول، فإذا مضى شهر فأبرأته من نفقة ما مضى وما يستقبل برىء مما مضى ومن شهر مما يستقبل وتمامه فيها اهـ.
And in al-Bahr from the chapter of judicial rulings: If you say, is the judge's assessment of maintenance a ruling from him? I say: It is a ruling, and asking for assessment requires a claim, so it exists after a claim and an incident. This is supported by what is in Nafaqat Khizanat al-Muftin: When the judge wants to assess maintenance, he says 'I have assessed upon you maintenance that does not drop your wife such and such' or 'I have assessed upon you maintenance for such a period' – it is valid and becomes obligatory upon the husband even after the period passes, because future maintenance becomes obligatory by the judge's ruling, so much so that if she waives after the assessment, it is valid. If you say: If he assesses for each day or each month, is it a ruling for all as long as the marriage lasts? I say: Yes, unless a preventing factor prevents it, as proven by what is in al-Khizanah: He assessed ten per month, and she waived her maintenance – he is free from the first month. Then when a month passes, she waives maintenance for the past and future – he is free from what passed and from one month of the future. And its completion is in it.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage clarifies that when a judge sets an amount of maintenance, it is a legal verdict (hukm) and not merely a recommendation. The requirement is that there be a claim (da'wa) and an incident (haditha). Once the judge pronounces the assessment, the husband becomes obliged to pay for future periods. Even future maintenance becomes immediately due by the judge's ruling. The example: if the judge assesses 10 dirhams per month, and the wife waives her right after assessment, she can only waive the first month's maintenance because that is the only one that has become a debt. Subsequent months' maintenance becomes due only as each month passes. The ruling stands for the entire duration of marriage unless a legal impediment arises (e.g., divorce).

Key Terms
تقدير (taqdīr): The judge's fixing of a specific amount of maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only when each month actually arrives.
B) Immediately upon the judge's ruling, even for future months.
C) Only if the wife requests each month.
D) Never; the husband can choose not to pay.

Key Takeaway
The judge's maintenance assessment is a binding verdict that makes future maintenance obligations effective from the moment of the ruling.

Items Included in Husband's Maintenance Obligation
Detailed list of maintenance items with disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the household items the husband must provide under maintenance and identify which expenses are disputed in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Maintenance extends beyond food and clothing to firewood, soap, oil, and water for ghusl—all related to marital intimacy.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Bahr al-Raiq expands the classical Hanafi definition of nafaqah (maintenance). While many assume maintenance is limited to food and clothing, the text enumerates several additional items the husband must provide, such as firewood, soap, potash, oil for lighting, and water for ghusl (full bath). It also addresses disputes, such as who bears the cost of water for wudu and the midwife's fee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The passage states that maintenance includes items that serve the purpose of marital intimacy (mūnat al-jimā‘). Recognize this as the underlying rationale for many of the listed items.
The phrase 'because it is for the expense of intercourse' appears multiple times.
2
List the Explicitly Mentioned Items
Extract each item mentioned: firewood, soap, potash (ushnān), oil for lighting and other uses, and the price of water for ghusl. These are clearly the husband's responsibility.
These items are all related to household maintenance and personal hygiene.
3
Note the Disputed Items
The passage introduces disagreements: water for wudu (some sources say it is on the husband; others say on the wife) and the midwife's fee (depends on hiring or not). Identify the positions and the basis for each.
The dispute arises from whether the item is necessary for the wife's personal worship (like wudu) or for marital companionship.
4
Examine the Rationale and Exceptions
Understand why water for ghusl is on the husband (directly related to intercourse), while water for wudu is debated. Also note the exception for bathhouse fees: the husband may prevent the wife from going if she is not postpartum, implying he must provide the water at home.
The principle is that the husband covers expenses arising from marriage, but not purely personal religious duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن النفقة التي على الزوج الحطب والصابون والأشنان والدهن للاستصباح وغيره وثمن ماء الاغتسال لأنه مؤنة الجماع. وفي كتاب رزين جعله عليها وفصل في ماء الطهر من الحيض بين أن يكون حيضها عشرة أيام فعليها أو أقل فعليه، وأجرة القابلة على من استأجرها من الزوجة والزوج، فإن جاءت بغير استئجار فلقائل أن يقول عليه لأنه مؤنة الجماع ولقائل أن يقول عليها كأجرة الطبيب وأما ثمن ماء الوضوء فعليها فإن كانت غنية تستأجر من ينقله ولا تنقله بنفسها، وإن كانت فقيرة فإما أن ينقله الزوج لها أو يدعها تنقله بنفسها؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وبه علم أن أجرة الحمام عليه لأنه ثمن ماء الاغتسال لكن له منعها من الحمام حيث لم تكن نفساء كما سيأتي بيانه. وسوى في الظهيرية بين ثمن ماء الاغتسال وماء الوضوء في الوجوب عليه وهو الظاهر وفي الواقعات ماء وضوئها عليه غنية كانت أو فقيرة لأنها لا بد لها منه فصار كالشرب ا.هـ.
And from the maintenance that is upon the husband: firewood, soap, potash, oil for lighting and other purposes, and the price of water for ghusl because it is for the expense of intercourse. In the book of Razin, he made it upon her and distinguished concerning water for purification from menstruation: if her menstruation is ten days, it is upon her; if less, upon him. The midwife's fee is upon whoever hired her, wife or husband. If she came without hiring, some say it is upon him because it is expense of intercourse, and some say it is upon her like a doctor's fee. As for the price of water for wudu, it is upon her. If she is wealthy, she hires someone to carry it and does not carry it herself; if she is poor, either the husband carries it for her or lets her carry it herself. As in al-Khulasa. And from this it is known that the fee of the bathhouse is upon him because it is the price of water for ghusl, but he may prevent her from the bathhouse if she is not in nifas (postpartum bleeding), as will come. And in al-Zahiriyya, he equated the price of water for ghusl and wudu in obligation upon him, and that is apparent. And in al-Waqi'at, water for her wudu is upon him whether she is rich or poor because she cannot do without it, so it becomes like drinking.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage expands the definition of maintenance (nafaqah) beyond food and clothing. The husband must provide firewood, soap, potash (cleaning agent), oil for lighting, and water for ghusl (full bath) because these are related to marital intimacy. There is a dispute about water for purification after menstruation: if her period is long (10 days), it's on her; if short, on him. The midwife's fee depends on who hired her; if no hiring, opinions differ. Water for wudu (ablution) is generally on the wife, but some sources say it is on the husband because she needs it daily, like drinking water. The wife cannot be forced to carry water if she is rich; the husband must provide it. The text also notes that the husband may prevent the wife from going to the public bathhouse (except after childbirth) because he must provide water at home.

Key Terms
أشنان (ushnān): Potash or alkali used for cleaning, similar to soap.
مؤنة الجماع (mūnat al-jimā‘): The expense of sexual intercourse; used as a rationale for items like water for ghusl.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance; financial support that a husband must provide to his wife.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Set-Off Between Husband's Debt and Wife's Maintenance
Ruling on set-off with exception for maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of set-off between a husband's debt and his wife's maintenance, explaining why the husband's consent is required.

Core Idea
Maintenance debt is weaker; it expires at death, so set-off needs the husband's consent.

When two parties owe each other debts of the same type, Hanafi law generally allows automatic set-off. However, a wife's maintenance debt is unique: it is extinguished upon her death, making it 'weaker' than the husband's debt, which survives. This lesson examines the ruling from al-Dhakhira that such set-off requires the husband's explicit consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two debts
Recognize the two claims: the wife's right to maintenance from her husband, and the husband's debt to the wife (e.g., from a loan). Both are monetary debts of the same type (dīn).
The debts must be of the same genus (e.g., both dirhams).
2
Recall the general rule of set-off
In Hanafi law, when two debts of the same type exist between two parties, they set off automatically without requiring consent. This is the default rule.
Automatic set-off extinguishes both debts to the extent of the smaller amount.
3
Spot the exception for maintenance
The text states that maintenance is an exception: set-off here requires the husband's consent. Note the conjunction 'except that' (إلا أن) signaling the departure from the general rule.
The reason is that maintenance debt is 'weaker' (أنقص) than other debts.
4
Understand why maintenance is weaker
Maintenance is extinguished if the wife dies; it does not become part of her estate. In contrast, a regular debt like a loan survives death and is payable from the estate. This makes the maintenance claim less durable, thus weaker.
Strength here refers to the persistence of the obligation after death.
5
Apply the ruling
If a husband seeks to set off his debt against his wife's maintenance, he must consent to the set-off. The wife cannot force automatic set-off. The husband's debt is stronger, so his permission is needed.
The same rule applies if one debt is 'sound' (good currency) and the other 'defective' (bad currency).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة: لو طلبت المرأة من القاضي فرض النفقة وكان للزوج عليها دين فقال احسبوا لها نفقتها منه كان له ذلك لأن الدينين من جنس واحد فتقع المقاصة كما في سائر الديون إلا أن في سائر الديون تقع مقاصة أو لم يتقاصا، وهنا يحتاج إلى رضا الزوج لوقوع المقاصة لأن دين النفقة أنقص من سائر الديون لسقوطه بالموت بخلاف سائر الديون فكان دين الزوج أقوى فيشترط رضاه بالمقاصة لو كان أحد الدينين جيداً والآخر رديئاً.
And in al-Dhakhira: If the woman asks the judge to assess maintenance, and the husband has a debt against her, and he says 'calculate her maintenance from it', he may do so because both debts are of the same type, so set-off occurs as in other debts. Except that in other debts, set-off occurs whether they explicitly set off or not. Here, the husband's consent is needed for set-off because the maintenance debt is weaker than other debts, as it is extinguished by death unlike other debts. So the husband's debt is stronger, thus his consent to set-off is required if one of the debts is sound and the other defective.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage discusses muqāṣṣa (set-off/compensation) between a husband's debt owed to the wife (e.g., a loan) and the wife's right to maintenance. Normally, when two debts of the same type exist between two parties, set-off occurs automatically without need for consent. However, maintenance is a special type of debt: it is extinguished if the wife dies, whereas other debts are not extinguished by death of the creditor. Therefore, the maintenance debt is considered 'weaker'. The husband's debt to her is 'stronger' because it persists even after her death (as it becomes part of her estate). Hence, set-off requires the husband's explicit consent; it does not happen automatically. The text also notes that if one debt is 'sound' (good quality, e.g., good currency) and the other 'defective' (e.g., bad currency), the same rule applies.

Key Terms
مقاصة (muqāṣṣa): Set-off or compensation between two debts of the same type.
دين النفقة (dayn al-nafaqa): Debt arising from the husband's obligation to provide maintenance to his wife.
رضا الزوج (riḍā al-zawj): Consent of the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does set-off between husband's debt and wife's maintenance require the husband's consent?
A) Because the husband is the stronger party in the marriage.
B) Because maintenance debt is extinguished by death, making it weaker.
C) Because the judge must approve all set-offs.
D) Because the wife's debt is smaller.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance debt is weaker than ordinary debts because it is extinguished upon death, so set-off with a stronger debt requires the consent of the stronger party.

Clothing (Kiswa) as Part of Maintenance: Amount and Custom
Details of clothing obligation and variation by custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the basic clothing items obligated in Hanafi maintenance and explain how custom modifies this obligation.

Core Idea
Clothing maintenance varies by custom – what is obligatory is determined by the norm (ma'ruf) of time and place.

In this passage, we learn that the obligation of clothing (kiswa) for a wife is not a fixed list; it adapts to customs and seasons. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani gave a minimum, but later scholars expanded based on local practice. The judge plays a key role in assessing sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
What items did Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani specify for annual clothing? Note the three items: two shifts (dir'), two headcovers (khimar), and one wrap (milhafa).
These are a minimum, not a rigid limit.
2
Examine the dispute over the milhafa
There is disagreement: some say it is an outer garment for going out, others say it is a night cover. This shows the early recognition of variability.
The dispute is about interpretation, not obligation.
3
Recognize the shift to custom
The author notes that in his own custom, trousers and additional items like jubbah and bedding are obligatory. This shows that the ruling expands beyond the baseline based on urf.
The phrase 'in our custom' signals that the obligation changes.
4
Understand the judge's role
According to al-Mujtaba, the judge must assess sufficiency (kifaya) according to the norm (ma'ruf) of the time and place. The judge can prescribe items or order their value.
The judge is the authority to adapt the ruling.
5
Apply the time frame
Clothing is normally prescribed every six months, but initial delay after marriage allows immediate demand. This is similar to maintenance in general.
Time frame can be adjusted based on need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكسوة فقال في الظهيرية: قدر محمد الكسوة بدرعين وخمارين وملحفة في كل سنة واختلفوا في تفسير الملحفة قال بعضهم الملاءة التي تلبسه المرأة عند الخروج. وقال بعضهم هي غطاء الليل تلبسه في الليل. وذكر درعين وخمارين أراد بهما صيفياً وشتوياً، ولم يذكر السراويل في الصيف ولا بد منه في الشتاء وهذا في عرفهم أما في عرفنا فتجب السراويل وثياب أخر كالجبة والفراش التي تنام عليه واللحاف وما تدفع به أذى الحر والبرد وفي الشتاء درع خز وجبة قز وخمار ابريسم. ولم يذكر الخف والمكعب في النفقة لأن ذلك إنما يحتاج إليه للخروج وليس للزوج تهيئة أسباب الخروج ا هـ. وفي المجتبى أن ذلك يختلف باختلاف الأماكن والعادات فيجب على القاضي اعتبار الكفاية بالمعروف في كل وقت ومكان، فإن شاء القاضي فرضها أصنافاً وإن شاء قومها وقضى بالقيمة. وفي الخلاصة وتفرض الكسوة كل ستة أشهر إلا إذا تزوج وبنى بها ولم يبعث إليها الكسوة لها أن تطالبه بالكسوة قبل مضي ستة أشهر والكسوة كالنفقة في أنه لا
As for clothing (kiswa), it is said in al-Zahiriyya: Muhammad assessed clothing as two shifts (dir'), two headcovers (khimar), and one wrap (milhafa) per year. They differed about the interpretation of milhafa: some said it is the outer garment the woman wears when going out; others said it is the night cover she wears at night. He mentioned two shifts and two headcovers, meaning one for summer and one for winter. He did not mention trousers in summer, but it is necessary in winter. This is in their custom. As for our custom, trousers are obligatory, and other garments like a jubbah, the bedding she sleeps on, the quilt, and what repels the harm of heat and cold. In winter, a silken shift, a silk jubbah, and a silk headcover. And he did not mention khuff (leather socks) and muka'ab (shoes) in maintenance because these are only needed for going out, and the husband is not required to provide means of going out. In al-Mujtaba: That differs according to places and customs, so the judge must consider sufficiency according to the norm (ma'ruf) in every time and place. The judge may prescribe them as items or estimate their value and order payment. In al-Khulasa: Clothing is prescribed every six months, except if he married and consummated and did not send her clothing, she may demand it before six months. Clothing is like maintenance in that it does not...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage details the kiswa (clothing) component of maintenance. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani set a minimum: two shifts (dir'), two headcovers (khimar), and one wrap (milhafa) per year. The nature of the milhafa is disputed: some say it is an outer garment for going out, others say a night cover. The two shifts and headcovers cover summer and winter needs. However, the text notes that customs differ. In the author's time, trousers and additional items like jubbah, bedding, and winter garments are obligatory. The judge should assess based on local custom and sufficiency. Clothing is normally due every six months, but if a husband delays initial clothing after consummation, she can demand it immediately. The last sentence about clothing being like maintenance is cut off, likely stating that it is also subject to similar rulings (e.g., waiver, past due).

Key Terms
ملحفة (milḥafa): A wrap or cover garment; interpreted as either a cloak for going out or a night cover.
الخف (al-khuff): Leather socks worn inside shoes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary determinant of the clothing obligation?
A) Imam Muhammad's list
B) Husband's wealth
C) Local custom (urf)
D) Wife's preference

Key Takeaway
Clothing maintenance is not a fixed list but adapts to time, place, and custom, with the judge ensuring sufficiency according to the norm.

Husband's Obligation for Wife's Washing Water
Ruling on husband's duty to provide water for ghusl and wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the husband's obligation to provide water for his wife's ghusl and wudu, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the qiyas used to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
The husband must provide water for his wife's ghusl and wudu because she cannot do without it, just as she cannot do without drinking water.

In the Hanafi school, the husband's maintenance (nafaqah) of his wife includes not only food and shelter but also water for her essential washing. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: is water for wudu (ablution) obligatory on the husband for a rich wife, or only for a poor one? By tracing the sources cited (al-Khulasa, al-Zahiriyya, al-Waqi'at) and the qiyas (analogy) to drinking water, you will understand why the stronger position obligates it in all cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
What exactly is the act being ruled upon? The husband providing water for his wife's ghusl and wudu. The ruling is that it is wajib (obligatory).
The passage also covers the bathhouse fee, which is part of the same obligation because it substitutes for water.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The passage does not cite a direct text from Quran or Sunnah but relies on qiyas (analogy). The asl (original case) is the obligation to provide drinking water; the far' (new case) is water for wudu and ghusl.
The operative analogy (wajh al-dalalah) is necessity: she cannot perform her religious duties without water for washing.
3
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Al-Khulasa restricted the obligation to a poor wife, while al-Waqi'at made it general for rich and poor. Al-Zahiriyya equated water for ghusl and wudu in obligation.
The conflicting positions come from later fatwa collections, not the primary texts (zahir al-riwayah).
4
Apply the Tarjih (Preference)
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) declares al-Waqi'at's position as 'apparent' (al-zahir) and notes the weakness of al-Khulasa. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that water for wudu is wajib on the husband regardless of the wife's wealth.
The revealing phrase is 'فصار كالشرب' (it becomes like drinking water), indicating a strong qiyas.
5
Identify Practical Consequences
The ruling extends to the bathhouse fee: the husband must pay it, but he may prevent the wife from going to the bathhouse unless she is in nifas (postpartum).
The husband's right to prevent is based on maqasid (privacy and modesty), but he must still provide water at home.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب النفقة كما في الخلاصة: وإن كانت فقيرة فإما أن ينقله الزوج لها أو تدعها تنقله بنفسها؛ كذا في وبه علم أن أجرة الحمام عليه لأنه ثمن ماء الاغتسال لكن له منعها من الحمام حيث لم تكن نفساء كما سيأتي بيانه. وسوى في الظهيرية بين ثمن ماء الاغتسال وماء الوضوء في الوجوب عليه وهو الظاهر وفي الواقعات ماء وضوئها عليه غنية كانت أو فقيرة لأنها لا بد لها منه فصار كالشرب ا.هـ. فظهر ضعف ما في الخلاصة.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Maintenance. As in al-Khulasa: If she is poor, then either the husband brings water for her or lets her bring it herself. It is known from this that the fee of the bathhouse is upon him because it is the price of the water for bathing (ghusl), but he can prevent her from the bathhouse when she is not in postpartum (nifas), as will come. And in al-Zahiriyya, it equates between the price of water for ghusl and water for wudu in obligation upon him, and that is apparent. In al-Waqi'at, the water for her wudu is upon him whether she is rich or poor because she cannot do without it, so it becomes like drinking water. End quote. So the weakness of what is in al-Khulasa becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage discusses the husband's obligation to provide water for his wife's washing. If the wife is poor, the husband must either bring water or allow her to bring it. The cost of the bathhouse is on the husband because it is payment for water for ghusl, but he can forbid her from going to the bathhouse unless she is in nifas (postpartum bleeding) because then she needs hot water. The text then compares water for ghusl and wudu: according to al-Zahiriyya, both are obligatory, and according to al-Waqi'at, water for wudu is always on the husband regardless of wealth. The passage concludes by noting that al-Khulasa's ruling is weak, as it did not make water for wudu obligatory for a rich wife.

Key Terms
الاغتسال (al-ghusl): Full body washing required after major ritual impurity (janabah, menstruation, etc.).
الوضوء (al-wudu): Ablution required for prayer.
الحمام (al-hammam): Public bathhouse; the husband is obliged to pay the fee for the wife's use if needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When she is in nifas (postpartum)
C) When she is poor
D) When she is rich

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates the husband to provide water for both ghusl and wudu, regardless of the wife's wealth, based on necessity and analogy to drinking water.

Offsetting Maintenance Debt Requires Husband's Consent
Ruling on offsetting maintenance debt with a debt the wife owes the husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on offsetting maintenance debt and explain why the husband's consent is required.

Core Idea
Maintenance debt lapses at death, so offsetting requires the husband's consent — unlike ordinary debts.

In Hanafi fiqh, offsetting (muqasah) between two debts of the same type is normally automatic. However, when one debt is maintenance owed to the wife, a special rule applies: the husband's consent is needed. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this exception from al-Dhakhira and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The text describes a wife asking the judge to fix maintenance while she owes a debt to her husband. The husband may request that the maintenance be offset from her debt. Note the ruling: offsetting is permissible, but only with the husband's consent.
2
State the general principle of muqasah
For ordinary debts of the same type, offsetting occurs automatically without the consent of the parties. This is the baseline rule in Hanafi law.
The text says 'as in other debts' initially, then modifies with the exception.
3
Identify the exception for maintenance debt
The text states that here, the husband's consent is needed. Identify why: because maintenance debt is 'inferior' (anqas) to other debts due to its lapsing upon death (suqutuhu bi al-mawt).
This lapsing means the debt is not inherited; it extinguishes if either spouse dies unpaid.
4
Explain the rationale (ta'lil)
The husband's debt is stronger because it survives death. Offsetting a strong debt against a weak one without consent would disadvantage the stronger party. Hence, consent is required to protect the husband's right.
The text analogizes to a case where one debt is good (jayyid) and the other bad (radī').
5
Note the practical consequence
If the husband consents, offsetting is valid and the wife's maintenance is considered paid. If he does not consent, the judge cannot force the offset; the husband must pay maintenance separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة: لو طلبت المرأة من القاضي فرض النفقة وكان للزوج عليها دين فقال احسبوا لها نفقتها منه كان له ذلك لأن الدينين من جنس واحد فتقع المقاصة كما في سائر الديون إلا أن في سائر الديون تقع تقاصاً أو لم يتقاصا، وهنا يحتاج إلى رضا الزوج لوقوع المقاصة لأن دین النفقة أنقص من سائر الديون لسقوطه بالموت بخلاف سائر الديون فكان دين الزوج أقوى فيشترط رضاه بالمقاصة لو كان أحد الدينين جيداً والآخر رديئاً !
In al-Dhakhira: If the woman asks the judge to fix maintenance and the husband has a debt against her, and he says 'Calculate her maintenance from it', he has that right because the two debts are of the same type, so offsetting occurs, as in other debts. Except that in other debts, offsetting occurs whether they consent or not, but here the husband's consent is needed for offsetting because the maintenance debt is inferior to other debts due to its lapsing upon death, unlike other debts. So the husband's debt is stronger, so his consent for offsetting is required, as if one debt is good and the other bad.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from al-Dhakhira deals with the scenario where a judge fixes maintenance for a wife who also owes a debt to her husband. The husband can request that the maintenance be offset against her debt because both are monetary obligations of the same type. However, unlike ordinary debts where offsetting is automatic, here the husband's consent is necessary because maintenance debt is weaker—it lapses at death (if not consumed), whereas ordinary debts do not. Therefore, the husband's claim is stronger, so his agreement is required for the offset. The analogy is made to a case where one debt is good and the other bad; similarly, the offset is not automatic without the stronger party's consent.

Key Terms
المقاصة (al-muqasah): Legal offsetting of two debts of the same type; in this context, setting off the maintenance debt against a debt the wife owes the husband.
دين النفقة (dayn al-nafaqah): The debt of maintenance that the husband owes to the wife, which is considered weaker than ordinary debts because it lapses upon death.
سقوطه بالموت (suqutuhu bi al-mawt): The lapsing of the maintenance debt upon the death of either spouse, meaning it is not inherited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the husband's consent matter for offsetting maintenance against the wife's debt?
A) Because the wife must agree
B) Because maintenance debt lapses upon death, making it weaker than other debts
C) Because the judge is not involved
D) Because the debts are not of the same type

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: offsetting maintenance debt against the wife's debt requires the husband's consent because maintenance debt lapses at death, making it weaker than the husband's surviving debt.

Guarantee for Wife's Maintenance: Abu Hanifa vs Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee duration for maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-school dispute on the duration of a guarantee for a wife's maintenance and apply the mufta bihi position to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A guarantee for future maintenance binds only one month (Abu Hanifa) or indefinitely (Abu Yusuf); the fatwa follows convenience.

This passage from Nafaqat al-Khassaf examines a practical difference within the Hanafi school: when a man guarantees his wife's ongoing maintenance but does not specify a duration, is he liable for one month or forever? The answer affects both the guarantor's financial risk and the wife's security. By studying this dispute, you will see how legal principles of strict construction vs. public benefit play out in a real ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is a guarantee (kafalah) of a wife's monthly maintenance. The dispute is over how long the guarantee binds: one month or indefinitely.
The guarantee is for a fixed monthly amount (ten dirhams) but without an explicit end date.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its underlying principle
Abu Hanifa holds that only one month is binding. His principle: a guarantee is a gratuitous contract and thus must be restricted to the literal scope of the promise.
This protects the guarantor from indefinite liability.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and its basis
Abu Yusuf holds that the guarantee is binding forever. His basis: the ongoing need for maintenance and the convenience of avoiding repeated renewals.
This is more practical for people and is the basis of the fatwa.
4
Identify the consensus on explicit wording
Both agree that if the guarantor says 'forever' or 'as long as you are married', the guarantee binds indefinitely as long as the marriage lasts.
This shows that the dispute only arises when no explicit duration is mentioned.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its significance
The text states the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view because it is more convenient. In practice, this is the operative ruling.
Knowing the mufta bihi helps a mufti or student give correct advice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نفقات الخصاف : لو كفل رجل لها بالنفقة كل شهر عشرة دراهم لزمه شهر واحد عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف يقع على الأبد وهو أرفق بالناس وعليه الفتوى وأجمعوا أنه لو قال كفلت لك بنفقتك كل شهر كذا أبداً أو ما دمتما زوجين فإنه يقع على الأبد ما داما زوجين.
In Nafaqat al-Khassaf: If a man guarantees for her maintenance of ten dirhams every month, then according to Abu Hanifa, one month is binding; according to Abu Yusuf, it is binding forever, and that is more convenient for the people, and the fatwa is upon it. And they are unanimous that if he says 'I guarantee your maintenance every month so-and-so forever' or 'as long as you are married', then it is binding forever as long as they are married.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage discusses the scope of a guarantee for future maintenance. Abu Hanifa limits the guarantee to one month because a guarantee is a gratuitous contract and should be construed narrowly. Abu Yusuf extends it indefinitely because the need is ongoing, and this is more convenient for people, so the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf. There is consensus that if the guarantor explicitly says 'forever' or 'as long as you are married', it applies indefinitely. This dispute shows how different principles—strict construction vs. public benefit—affect rulings.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A guarantee or surety; a contract where one person assumes liability for another's obligation.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance: food, clothing, shelter, and other necessities for the wife.
أبو حنيفة (Abu Hanifa): The founder of the Hanafi school; his view is that the guarantee is limited to one month.
أبو يوسف (Abu Yusuf): A leading student of Abu Hanifa; his view is the guarantee is forever.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On which point is there consensus (ijma') according to the passage?
A) That the guarantee is binding forever regardless of wording
B) That if the guarantor says 'forever', it is binding forever
C) That Abu Hanifa's view is correct
D) That the guarantee is only for one month

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi is that a guarantee for a wife's maintenance is binding indefinitely unless the guarantor explicitly limits it, but there is consensus that explicit 'forever' wording makes it permanent.

Clothing Maintenance (Kiswah): Definition and Timing
Ruling on definition and timing of wife's clothing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kiswah, list the default clothing items provided by Imam Muhammad, explain the timing of payment and the role of custom.

Core Idea
Kiswah is wajib; its items and timing are flexible based on custom, with a default of two gowns, two headcovers, and a wrap annually.

This passage from the commentary defines what clothing a husband must provide for his wife. While Imam Muhammad estimated a specific list based on the customs of his time, the Hanafi school emphasizes that such details can change with place and culture. You will learn the default items, the timing (every six months, with earlier demand possible), and the husband's right to enforce wearing because her adornment is his right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its obligation level
The passage begins by addressing kiswah (clothing maintenance). It is wajib on the husband, separate from nafaqah but similar in rules.
Note the phrase 'وأما الكسوة' introducing the topic.
2
Memorize Imam Muhammad's default list
Imam Muhammad estimated two gowns (dir'), two headcovers (khimar), and one wrap (milhafa) per year, with summer and winter variants.
The disagreement on milhafa shows custom's role.
3
Recognize the flexibility of the ruling
Later scholars note that customs change: trousers, silk garments, and bedding are added based on need. The judge should apply 'kifayah bi al-ma'ruf' (sufficiency by recognized norms).
Al-Mujtaba: 'يختلف باختلاف الأماكن والعادات'
4
Understand the timing and enforcement
Kiswah is due every six months, but can be demanded earlier after consummation if the husband delays. The husband can raise the wife to the judge to wear the clothing, as her adornment is his right.
Also, if she starves herself, he can intervene.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on basis
Some (al-Khassaf) say kiswah considers both spouses' status; others consider only the husband's. This affects the amount but not the obligation.
The mufta bihi is flexibility per custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكسوة فقال في الظهيرية : قدر محمد الكسوة بدرعين وخمارين وملحفة في كل سنة واختلفوا في تفسير الملحفة قال بعضهم الملاءة التي تلبسه المرأة عند الخروج. وقال بعضهم هي غطاء الليل تلبسه في الليل. وذكر درعين وخمارين أراد بهما صيفياً وشتوياً، ولم يذكر السراويل في الصيف ولا بد منه في الشتاء وهذا في عرفهم أما في عرفنا فتجب السراويل وثياب أخر كالجبة والفراش التي تنام عليه واللحاف وما تدفع به أذى الحر والبرد وفي الشتاء درع خز وجبة قز وخمار ابريسم. ولم يذكر الخف والمكعب في النفقة لأن ذلك إنما يحتاج إليه للخروج وليس للزوج تهيئة أسباب الخروج ا هـ. وفي المجتبى أن ذلك يختلف باختلاف الأماكن والعادات فيجب على القاضي اعتبار الكفاية بالمعروف في كل وقت ومكان، فإن شاء القاضي فرضها أصنافاً وإن شاء قومها وقضى بالقيمة. وفي الخلاصة وتفرض الكسوة كل ستة أشهر إلا إذا تزوج وبنى بها ولم يبعث إليها الكسوة لها أن تطالبه بالكسوة قبل مضي ستة أشهر والكسوة كالنفقة في أنه لا يشترط مضي المدة وللزوج أن يرفعها إلى القاضي حتى يأمرها بلبس الثوب لأن الزينة حقه اهـ. وهو يدل على أن المرأة لو أمسكت النفقة وأكلت قليلاً وقترت على نفسها فله أن يرفعها إلى القاضي لتأكل بما فرض لها خوفاً عليها من الهزال فإنه يضره. وفي غاية البيان معزياً إلى الخصاف: ويجعل لها ما تنام عليه مثل الفراش ومضربة ومرقعة في الشتاء ولحافاً تتغطى به . قال شمس الأئمة في شرح كتاب النفقات : ذكر لها فراشاً على حدة ولم يكتف بفراش واحد لأنها ربما تعتزل عنه في أيام الحيض أو في زمان مرضها وفي فتح القدير : ذكر في الأصل الدرع من الكسوة والخصاف ذكر القميص وهما سواء إلا أن القميص يكون مجيباً من قبل الكتف والدرع من قبل الصدر. هـ. وفي البدائع الكسوة على الاختلاف كالنفقة من اعتبار حاله فقط أو حالهما على قول الخصاف.
As for clothing, in al-Zahiriyya: Muhammad estimated the clothing at two gowns (dir'), two headcovers (khimar), and one wrap (milhafa) every year. They disagreed on the interpretation of milhafa. Some said it is the outer garment worn when going out. Some said it is the night cover worn at night. He mentioned two gowns and two headcovers, intending summer and winter. He did not mention trousers in summer, but they are necessary in winter. This is in their custom. As for our custom, trousers and other garments like a gown (jubba), the bedding she sleeps on, the quilt (lihaf), and what repels the harm of heat and cold are obligatory. In winter, a silk gown (dir' khazz), a silk jubba (jubba qazz), and a silk headcover (khimar ibrisam). He did not mention socks (khuff) and shoes (muka'ab) in maintenance because those are only needed for going out, and the husband is not obliged to provide means of going out. End quote. In al-Mujtaba: This varies by place and custom; the judge should consider sufficiency in accordance with recognized norms at every time and place. If the judge wishes, he can fix it in kind, or he can assess their value and give the value. In al-Khulasa: Clothing is fixed every six months, except if he marries and consummates the marriage and does not send clothing, she can demand it before six months. Clothing is like maintenance in that the passage of time is not a condition, and the husband can raise her to the judge to order her to wear the garment because her adornment is his right. End quote. This indicates that if the woman hoards the maintenance and eats little and stints herself, he can raise her to the judge to make her eat what was fixed for her, fearing harm from emaciation. In Ghayat al-Bayan quoting al-Khassaf: He provides for her what she sleeps on, like a mattress, a pillow, and a quilt in winter, and a blanket to cover herself. Shams al-A'imma in Sharh Kitab al-Nafaqat said: He mentioned a separate mattress for her, not content with one mattress, because she may separate from him during her menses or illness. In Fath al-Qadir: Al-Asl mentioned a gown (dir') for clothing, and al-Khassaf mentioned a shirt (qamis), and they are the same except that the shirt is opened from the shoulder and the gown from the chest. End quote. In al-Bada'i': Clothing follows the difference like maintenance: either considering his financial status only, or both, according to al-Khassaf's view.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from the commentary defines the clothing maintenance (kiswah) a husband must provide. Imam Muhammad estimated certain items annually, but this is based on the customs of his time. The judge must consider local customs and sufficiency. Clothing is due every six months, and the wife can demand it earlier if the husband delays after consummation. The husband can enforce wearing because her adornment is his right. The text also includes bedding and distinctions between types of garments. The ruling is flexible: if the judge wishes, he can fix the clothing in kind or provide its value. The passage also touches on the case where the wife starves herself, giving the husband the right to intervene for her health. Finally, there is a note that clothing, like maintenance, may be based on the husband's financial status alone or on both spouses' status, per al-Khassaf.

Key Terms
الكسوة (al-kiswah): Clothing maintenance; the garments and bedding a husband must provide for his wife.
الملحفة (al-milhafa): A wrap or cover; interpreted as an outer garment or a night cover.
الفراش (al-firash): Bedding or mattress; the husband must provide a separate mattress for the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can the wife demand clothing before the six-month period?
A) If her husband is absent
B) If the husband married her and consummated but did not send clothing
C) If the clothing is lost
D) If the judge orders it

Key Takeaway
Kiswah is a flexible wajib obligation that adapts to custom, with a default list but ultimate authority given to the judge to ensure sufficiency.
